What David Senra Learned Studying 400+ Founders

Sequoia Capital(211.0K) ·56:52 ·2.8K views · 2026-06-04
David Senra (Host, Founders Podcast)
The definitive trait shared by history’s greatest founders is an absolute, obsessive focus—the ability to "mute the world and build your own"—driven by a compulsive need for control and a deep founder-problem fit, rather than a desire for wealth or a well-balanced life.
TL;DR The definitive trait shared by history’s greatest founders is an absolute, obsessive focus—the ability to "mute the world and build your own"—driven by a compulsive need for control and a deep founder-problem fit, rather than a desire for wealth or a well-balanced life. ...

TL;DR

The definitive trait shared by history’s greatest founders is an absolute, obsessive focus—the ability to "mute the world and build your own"—driven by a compulsive need for control and a deep founder-problem fit, rather than a desire for wealth or a well-balanced life.

1. The Power of Absolute Focus and Obsession

  • Muting the World: David Senra distills the single common trait of legendary founders into "focus," which he describes as an extreme, almost different-species-level of ignoring outside noise.
      • The Dana White Example: White purchased the UFC for $2 million, lost money for six years (putting in an additional $40 million of partner capital), and eventually built it into an organization with an $8 billion TV rights deal. He succeeded by operating purely as a passionate fan, never reading a business book or listening to a business podcast, effectively shutting out external conventional wisdom.
  • The Discipline of "No": Focus is not merely avoiding bad ideas, but actively saying no to good ideas that distract from the great ones.
      • Senra references Jony Ive’s recollection of Steve Jobs, who defined focus as the painful act of turning down highly desirable initiatives because they interfered with the core mission.

2. Founder-Problem Fit and Behavioral Archetypes

  • Founder-Problem Fit Over Product-Market Fit: Spotify founder Daniel Ek and Senra are developing a framework of founder archetypes to help leaders avoid the trap of destructive imitation (e.g., trying to copy Steve Jobs or Elon Musk).
      • Ek believes "founder-problem fit" is far more critical than product-market fit or founder-industry fit. A prime example is Demis Hassabis of DeepMind, who was uniquely built for that singular mission.
  • The Father-Son Dynamic: A recurring biographical pattern in historical founders is the profound psychological impact of the father-son relationship. Senra references the maxim, "You can always understand the son by the story of his father."
      • Francis Ford Coppola: Driven to an extreme work ethic by a brilliant but failed musician father who told him, "There can only be one genius in the family, and it's me."
      • John D. Rockefeller: Shaped by a father who was a bigamist and a scam artist who actively tried to cheat his own sons.
  • The "Imitation Deficit" Advantage: Tech founders often exhibit traits on the autism/Asperger’s spectrum, which Peter Thiel notes can be a massive advantage.
      • This condition often leaves individuals missing the "imitation socialization gene," preventing them from being talked out of highly unconventional, original ideas (like SpaceX) by social pressure.

3. The Myth of the Well-Balanced Life

  • A Brutal Statistical Reality: Out of more than 400 founders studied by Senra, only three maintained what could be considered a healthy, well-balanced life.
      • The three exceptions are mathematician and quantitative hedge fund pioneer Ed Thorp, retail giant Sol Price, and luxury fashion designer Brunello Cucinelli.
  • Compulsion Over Regret: Legendary founders are rarely motivated by money; instead, they are driven by a compulsive need for control and the desire to build.
      • Sam Walton: Wrote in his autobiography while dying of cancer that despite sacrificing immense family time, he would do it the exact same way if given another chance.
      • Phil Knight: Reflected in *Shoe Dog* on his grief after his son's tragic drowning, yet admitted his biggest regret was that he could not go back and build Nike all over again.
  • Shifting the Fuel Source: Many successful founders begin with a dark, negative inner drive (fear, anger, or a chip on the shoulder). Senra notes that to survive long-term, founders must transition their motivation from a punishing self-talk model to a generative model.
      • Billionaire entrepreneur Brad Jacobs (who built eight separate billion-dollar companies) realized that relentless self-punishment stops serving a founder once they discover their true life's work.

4. Organizational Design and the AI Platform Shift

  • The Age of Infinite Leverage: Michael Dell and Shopify's Tobi Lütke agree that the current AI-native shift is fundamentally different from previous platform changes (SaaS, mobile, internet) because of the extreme leverage it provides.
      • Organizations will require far fewer people, and planning cycles will dramatically compress.
      • In this era, top-tier talent will not be 2x or 10x more valuable, but up to 10,000x more valuable, deserving proportional compensation.
  • The Fallacy of Co-Founders: While partnerships exist (e.g., Warren Buffett and Charlie Munger, or Andrew Carnegie and operator Henry Clay Frick), historical data shows that companies are almost always powered by a single, dominant driving force. Co-founding teams rarely survive the test of time, and the secondary partner almost always exits.

Notable Quotes

"Mute the world and then build your own." — David Senra
"Focus is saying no to a good idea that you really want to do because it distracts you from a great idea." — Steve Jobs *(as quoted by David Senra)*
"If you pretend to be in control, everybody else will assume that you are." — Nolan Bushnell *(advice given to Steve Jobs, as quoted by David Senra)*
"If you give a mediocre idea to a great team, they’ll either fix it or throw it out and create something new." — Ed Catmull *(co-founder of Pixar, as quoted by David Senra)*

Munger had a big ego, and when I met him, he told me the funniest thing. I'm actually surprised to hear that. I thought he subjugated his ego a lot. Well, he talks about this, and he used that exact word, subjugate. He said, "If I could do life again, I always thought I was smarter than everybody else. If I could do life again, I'd still think I was smarter than everybody else, but I would do a better job of hiding it." Okay. His point was, "I thought I was really smart, but I recognized the talent that Buffett had that I didn't. Buffett was more singularly focused, whereas I wanted to fish, boat, design architecture, do real estate, and do all this other stuff. How arrogant would I have to be to not subjugate myself to a once-in-a-century talent?" Got it. His perspective was that Buffett let him shape his mind and thinking, but he realized Buffett was the driving engine behind their success. One of my ahas, though, is that I don't think there are a lot of co-founding teams that lasted the test of time. I think it's pretty rare, and I see that today in Silicon Valley. It's usually one dominant person. Yeah, and then the others go away. The second person leaves—Wozniak went away, and the co-founders of Facebook went away. It's pretty common. That was my argument. Maybe that one person needed those other people at the beginning, but there's always just one singular driving force behind the company. Let's talk about these individuals. You get inside their heads. Some people have positive self-talk, while others, like me, have negative self-talk. Still? Yes. I just changed this about myself. It is the best ever. I've tried. I can't think of anybody right now who has imposter syndrome. Do you still have it? Yeah, I feel kind of nervous right now. Why are you nervous? I just am. I was just in a board meeting for the last three hours, and I'm sitting there like, "What am I doing here?" Interesting. One of the greatest pieces of advice Steve Jobs ever got was from Nolan Bushnell, the founder of Atari, who was about nine or ten years older than him. He was his mentor and told Steve, "If you pretend to be in control, everybody else will assume that you are." Steve took that advice and ran with it. So, I don't know about imposter syndrome. You brought up something interesting about platform changes. The Industrial Revolution was a huge platform change, and Henry Ford's assembly line was another massive one. When those things happened, did the profile of the founder CEO change? And did the way they ran their companies change? We're going through something now that is probably even bigger. What do you mean by the profile changing? Paul Graham talked about "founder mode" versus "manager mode." Manager mode is like Jack Welch—military-style, top-down, organizational structure. Founder mode is like Brian Chesky—distrustful of conventional wisdom, thinner layers of management, getting right down to the details. Those are the two modes I see CEOs in today, with people tilting toward founder mode. In the Dorsey interview, Jack Dorsey talked about "Dorsey mode," where he gets rid of the org chart and titles. More decisions are made by an AI system in the middle of the company, and people are just in charge of giving context and judgment. Over time, the percentage of decisions the AI makes versus humans starts to flip. I call it Dorsey mode—a new way to build a company with far fewer people than previous generations needed. Did something similar happen to the way they built companies during previous platform shifts? That's a great question, and I actually just asked Michael Dell this. Think about Dell: he started his company when he was 19 with $1,000 in his University of Texas dorm room, deciding to take on IBM, the biggest company in the world. It was an absolute batshit crazy idea. Most people don't know that IBM was the first company in history to hit a $100 billion market cap, holding 80% market share of the entire tech industry. Marc Andreessen told me on my podcast that it was like having ten Googles. We haven't seen that level of domination since. Michael Dell succeeded, and Dell was actually profitable every single quarter for the first 20 years of its existence. I'm always skeptical when people say "this time is different," because everybody in history says that. Michael has navigated many platform shifts and technological revolutions, so I asked him because this current shift feels different to me. He said, "No, this is not like anything else I've been through. I actually think the way to run a company is completely different." Tobi Lütke believes this, too. The options available to you now are completely different because of the amount of leverage. I think the shape of the organization changes, the number of people you need changes, the way you compensate 100x people who are proficient with these tools changes, and the planning cycles change because things move so fast. I actually think it's different compared to when the internet, Google, SaaS, and mobile came along. This is a lot different. There is a great line in "The Almanac of Naval Ravikant" where he says, "In the age of infinite leverage, being at the extreme of your craft is very important." That was written before AI, and AI is just leverage. That's why the premium on focus is so high. Just trying to be the best in the world at whatever you do is going to reap so many more rewards. Being at the extreme edge of your craft is unbelievably valuable. We've already seen that talented people aren't just 100 times more valuable; they are like 1,000 times more valuable. I'm really close to John and Jordy of The Yard, and I'm obsessed with podcasters. Everybody is trying to clone them, but they can't clone their minds. You are going to see people like that in marketing, research, and everywhere else whom you should pay 10,000x. I was always a little bit nervous. I liked what David had to say about it. I liked even more what Brian Chesky has to say about it. When he was early in Airbnb, he kind of led from a position of fear. He had deep, deep imposter syndrome, and Brian has done a lot of work on himself and now he leads from a position of love. I'm trying to be more like Brian. Like what I love is helping CEOs and founders like yourself go on that journey from early stage startup founder to big behemoth legendary scale-up CEO, and I'm trying to lead from a position of love versus a position of fear. I recommend you do the same. Hope you liked the episode, you can follow me @BHealy_ on X and we can chat about it more.

How a reasoning model cracked an 80-year-old math problem — the OpenAI Podcast Ep. 20

OpenAI(2.0M) ·41:18 ·5.8K views · 2026-06-04
Alexander Wei (Reasoning Researcher, OpenAI) Hongxun Wu (Reasoning Researcher, OpenAI) Lijie Chen (Reasoning Researcher, OpenAI)
An OpenAI general-purpose reasoning model has disproved Paul Erdős’s 80-year-old unit distance conjecture.
TL;DR An OpenAI general-purpose reasoning model has disproved Paul Erdős’s 80-year-old unit distance conjecture. This breakthrough, driven by "test-time compute" (allowing the model to think longer before responding), highlights how AI reasoning can solve major open scientific...

TL;DR

An OpenAI general-purpose reasoning model has disproved Paul Erdős’s 80-year-old unit distance conjecture. This breakthrough, driven by "test-time compute" (allowing the model to think longer before responding), highlights how AI reasoning can solve major open scientific problems and act as a powerful catalyst for human-AI collaborative research.

Cracking the Erdős Unit Distance Conjecture

  • The Conjecture: Formulated by legendary mathematician Paul Erdős, this 80-year-old problem in combinatorial geometry asks for the maximum number of point-pairs that can be exactly one inch apart in a set of $n$ points on a plane. Erdős conjectured that arranging the points in a square grid was asymptotically optimal.
  • The Disproof: The OpenAI model proved that the square grid is not optimal and that a far superior arrangement is possible.
      • Methodology: The model made a highly creative leap, bridging two historically distinct fields by applying *class field theory* (an advanced branch of number theory) to a problem in combinatorial geometry.
      • Significance: This was a central problem in discrete geometry, carrying a historic $500 bounty from Erdős. The resulting proof was described by researchers as "journal-publishable" and far beyond International Mathematical Olympiad (IMO) difficulty.
  • A Generalist Success: The breakthrough was achieved using a general-purpose reasoning model (the predecessor to OpenAI o1), not a specialized model trained solely on mathematics.

The Power of Test-Time Compute

  • Inference-Time Scaling: Unlike previous AI models that responded instantly, reasoning models utilize "test-time compute"—allowing them to think, execute Python code to test hypotheses, self-correct, and search through different reasoning pathways before outputting an answer.
  • A Direct Correlation to Accuracy: The researchers noted a distinct scaling curve. When given more time to think, the model's accuracy on the Erdős problem rose significantly, eventually reaching a ~50% success rate under high compute budgets.
  • Blistering Pace of Progress: Just 10 months prior to this breakthrough, researchers were debating whether AI could win a gold medal at the International Math Olympiad by 2026. The model achieved that milestone in June 2024, and Olympiad-level math is now considered "in the rearview mirror" of AI capability.

Verification and Immediate Community Impact

  • The Verification Process: Researchers Hongxun Wu and Lijie Chen first ran the problem on internal models. After the model self-verified, they handed the proof to math researchers within OpenAI (including contributors Matb and Maxelki).
      • Initially skeptical, the human mathematicians spent four to five days searching for bugs before concluding the proof was entirely correct.
      • The model's output included a highly complex, 125-page chain-of-thought process.
  • No Scaffolding Required: The model generated the proof entirely natively from a direct prompt, without the need for external logical scaffolding, steering, or specialized theorem-proving languages like Lean.
  • Immediate Scientific Acceleration: Within a week of OpenAI sharing the result, external mathematicians digested the model's novel methodology and successfully used its intuition to disprove the *sum-product conjecture for real numbers*, demonstrating an immediate multiplier effect on human mathematical output.

A New Paradigm for Human-AI Collaboration

  • Empowerment Over Displacement: The researchers emphasize that AI will not render mathematicians obsolete. While AI is highly effective at creative problem-solving and connecting distant mathematical ideas, humans remain essential for synthesizing these results, building new frameworks, and formulating high-level theories.
  • Methodology for Researchers: The team shared key strategies for scientists looking to integrate reasoning models into their workflows:
      • Trust Calibration: Lijie Chen recommends "exponentially doubling your trust" in the model month-by-month until it fails, then stepping back slightly, to discover the true limits of its capabilities.
      • Avoid Over-Decomposition: Rather than manually breaking down a problem into smaller steps, researchers should prompt the model with the end-goal directly. Human decomposition often introduces personal biases and blind spots, whereas the AI may find a completely novel shortcut.
  • Broad Scientific Horizon: The team expects this reasoning paradigm to rapidly accelerate other fields:
      • Cryptography: Stress-testing the mathematical foundations of security protocols to prove safety or find hidden vulnerabilities.
      • Quantum Computing: Using AI to discover more efficient quantum error-correcting codes, accelerating physical implementations of quantum hardware.

Notable Quotes

"Everyone had a hard time sleeping because it's so exciting... This is something that can be published in the best journal of math. We knew this day was going to come, but never knew that it was going to be reality so fast. It's like living in a dream." — Lijie Chen, Reasoning Researcher, OpenAI
"I think the reaction was very positive. I think people really get a sense that the frontier of AI today can really come up with research output that many human mathematicians would be proud to achieve." — Alexander Wei, Reasoning Researcher, OpenAI
"You could be an astronomer and not use a telescope, but you kind of have to ask why... We do math not just to enjoy the pleasure of problem-solving, but also to advance the field and to understand the truth we're looking for. Using AI is going to speed that up by a lot." — Hongxun Wu, Reasoning Researcher, OpenAI

Oh, I wanted to say P versus NP too, but Hongxun said it. I think because models now are very, very good at coming up with some idea to solve a problem, but they are not good at proposing a completely new, different kind of math, or proposing a new theory. How to get them to do that is still very, very open. How I would think about it is, we see this Moore's law for the time horizon at which these models are effective, and I think you sort of feel that in math. Every few months, the amount of time these models can work independently doubles—at least the amount of human-equivalent time. So, for solving problems, if you're really good at it, maybe some problems actually have pretty short paths to the solution and you don't need to take that long. But I think for inventing new ways of doing mathematics, that's much more like a years- or decades-long process, and so I think it'll still take a bit of time for that exponential to get there. This was done by an internal model that you guys worked on, and since then, o1 has been able to do the same thing, and we've seen other labs say they've been able to do this as well. But this was several weeks ago, which is now ancient history. What have we seen since then? I think one difference between the original result and the follow-up findings is that, for the original model, there was no scaffolding needed. You sort of just asked it to do the problem, and then it gave you the answer. You can read the original prompt and response in the note we uploaded on the blog, whereas I think the follow-up efforts have had a little bit more structure or steering of the models. But I think one interesting data point here is that it's really all about test-time compute scaling. After we initially solved the problem—and this is the plot Lijie brought up earlier—with enough test-time compute budget, the model is able to solve the problem around 50% of the time. So, it's not surprising that you can get there with other methods as well. But I think what's really important here is just that as you pour in more test-time compute, you get better results. It seems like it's a virtuous cycle, where you take today's model, give it more compute, let it solve, and understand how these points can be solved. Next-generation models can learn from that and just get more and more efficient. It basically seems like it just scales forever, right? What do you think we're going to see by the end of the year? What I want to see is people using our model to discover lots of new stuff, not only in math, but also in all of science. Of course, OpenAI wants to do some cool math stuff, but I think it would be better if everyone can use the model to discover their own science. And I would expect many mathematicians will use the model—not completely reliant on the model, but collaborating with the model—to discover a lot more math results. I think that would be really cool. Hongxun, I've talked to some mathematicians who are very reticent to even try using AI in mathematics. What is the best argument you can give? I think I'll just show them the proof—the disproof of the unit distance conjecture. I think it is just about productivity, right? We do math not just to enjoy the pleasure of problem-solving, but also to advance the field and to understand the truth that we're looking for. Using AI is going to speed that up by a lot. It's going to tell us what we are really struggling to find, and it will be hard to resist using AI at some point. You could be an astronomer and not use a telescope, but you kind of have to ask why. Yeah, exactly. I know one of the researchers here likes to watch computers play chess against each other, and it feels like he sometimes learns things from that. Do you think that we'll learn to be better mathematicians or researchers or scientists, or just thinkers in general, by watching the solutions the models come up with? Looking at the 125 pages of thinking is probably not very helpful for a mathematician. But just by looking at the answer, you actually do learn some idea that was not there before—that you didn't know before—and that inspires later mathematical works that knock down other problems. So, I definitely think people learn; mathematicians learn something from AI solutions. Some of the mathematicians we asked to review the proof, together with their collaborators, actually used the idea to disprove the sum-product conjecture, but for real numbers. I think that's one very good example: AI can crack down important questions and give us ideas that we can apply elsewhere. Yeah, and I think it's remarkable that this group of mathematicians has already, just in the span of a week, used it to disprove this result, which is of similar importance to the unit distance conjecture. This is a wonderful example of mathematicians seeing this, using it as inspiration, and bringing the ideas to bear on a different problem. What does this mean for the mathematical community? I think for us, when we do these experiments, we want to make sure we empower the academic communities we interact with. We don't want to just go to some community from the outside, try to solve a bunch of their problems, and give them a bunch of AI slop. What we really want to do is make these tools available to researchers and let them direct all this AI test-time compute at the problems they think are important. I don't think we should be viewed as running a race to solve as many Erdős problems as we can, but more like we want to make people aware that the technology is out there and this is what it can do. You're not trying to solve every Erdős problem. Yeah, I would not say that is our goal. This just happened to be a particularly significant result that we thought would be important to share with the world to show the capability level of models today. But it's really not the goal to just go through the list as if it were a race. Do you foresee things applying to cryptography? There's also some debate about whether these models get so good that we kind of surpass even where quantum computing goes, which sounds kind of crazy. Yeah, I think cryptography is really an important topic these days because the foundation of cryptography is really about some problems—like factoring—being hard to solve by computers, but basically, we only have conjectures. There's no mathematical proof of this fact. And suppose the model gets really good at algorithms. Maybe they will prove some of the cryptography conjectures and say, "Okay, those protocols are actually secure; we don't have to conjecture them to be secure." Or maybe they'll find some loophole, which is also very important, because we need to make sure the foundation of our security is good. The model can stress test the foundation of the cryptography to make sure we have better security. What about quantum computing? I think that's a very different territory. Actually, I used to study quantum computing—my first paper was on quantum advantage, which showed that for some tasks, quantum computers can do better than classical computers. But so far, the models are just classical computers. They do what humans can do, maybe a bit better. But quantum computers can sometimes do fancier stuff, like simulating quantum effects in chemistry, which we probably... okay, I'm not an expert on that, but it's unclear. They are just two different paradigms, so I'm not super sure how they compare to each other. But I think AI is going to greatly accelerate the pace at which we develop quantum computers. In recent years, there's been improvement in things like error-correcting—like quantum error-correcting codes that only use simpler types of operations—and that really speeds up the physical implementation. So, I expect more of this to come from collaboration with AI, where AI can propose new quantum error correction algorithms, and then we can develop quantum computers much faster. Once you ask a model to solve a question, you can, of course, follow up with, "How did you solve it?" or "Can you explain this part of the proof to me?" Then the model will patiently try to teach you how everything goes, line by line. It's actually not just one-shot problem-solving; you can ask it follow-up questions to learn how the proof works, and I really like that. One thing you learn very quickly as a researcher is that if your results are too good to be true, you probably have a bug somewhere. I think every researcher has had experiences where they see amazing numbers from their experiment, and it turns out the experiment was actually wrong and the numbers were wrong. When I first heard about this from Lijie and Hongxun, that was my prior. I was like, "Oh, I'll wait for them to find the bug." But then, as the days went on, you sort of have this growing optimism that, maybe, this is the one in a hundred times where it's too good to be true, but it is actually true. Well, gentlemen, thank you very much. Yeah, thanks so much. Thank you so much.

Fed's Daly Says Forward Guidance Could Be Misleading

Bloomberg Technology(727.0K) ·14:45 ·172 views · 2026-06-04
Mary Daly (President, Federal Reserve Bank of San Francisco)
Federal Reserve Bank of San Francisco President Mary Daly remains highly bullish on AI's long-term economic potential despite a current lack of macro productivity gains, while cautioning that elevated economic uncertainty makes near-term interest rate forward guidance potentially misleading.
TL;DR Federal Reserve Bank of San Francisco President Mary Daly remains highly bullish on AI's long-term economic potential despite a current lack of macro productivity gains, while cautioning that elevated economic uncertainty makes near-term interest rate forward guidance...

TL;DR

Federal Reserve Bank of San Francisco President Mary Daly remains highly bullish on AI's long-term economic potential despite a current lack of macro productivity gains, while cautioning that elevated economic uncertainty makes near-term interest rate forward guidance potentially misleading.

AI Adoption, Productivity, and Financial Stability

  • The Productivity Lag: Widespread, transformative productivity gains from AI have not yet materialized in macroeconomic data.
      • Litmus Test Timing: Businesses are actively transforming their internal processes, with many citing next year as the true "litmus test" for realized return on investment (ROI).
      • Beyond Tech: Daly is particularly bullish on non-tech sector investments. Examples include machining companies training AI on 50 years of technical blueprints and robotics firms optimizing shipping logistics.
  • No Financial Stability Risks: Despite high valuations and market enthusiasm, Daly does not see current AI investments posing systemic financial stability risks.
      • Capital Sources: Much of the current capital expenditure is funded directly by mega-cap technology companies (the "Magnificent Seven") rather than highly leveraged bank debt.
      • Systemic Spread: For a market dip to trigger a financial stability concern, it would need to spread directly to consumers, businesses, and the banking sector—which is not occurring.
  • Differences from the Dot-Com Era: While San Francisco is experiencing enthusiasm reminiscent of 1996, the current AI wave is far more pervasive and integrated into core business operations than the dot-com boom.

Monetary Policy and the Risks of Forward Guidance

  • Misleading Guidance: Daly warns that providing explicit forward guidance on interest rates right now is "misguided" due to an unusually high number of economic crosscurrents.
  • Geopolitical Disruptions: Geopolitical tensions (such as conflicts impacting oil and fertilizer prices) present ongoing inflation risks that could counter the disinflationary potential of technology.
      • Oil Projections: Despite current volatility, the futures market projects oil to stabilize around $80 a barrel by the end of the year.
  • Policy Readiness: Current monetary policy is well-positioned to react flexibly to economic data as it evolves in either direction. Daly’s top priority remains returning inflation to the Fed's target.

Labor Market Stabilization and Hiring Caution

  • Stabilized Conditions: The U.S. labor market has stabilized following supportive policy adjustments made late last year.
  • AI-Driven Hiring Hesitancy: Businesses are displaying extreme caution regarding adding headcount.
      • Agent Evaluation: Rather than aggressively hiring, firms are actively evaluating what tasks AI agents can perform first to avoid overhiring and subsequent painful layoffs.
      • Skill Alignment: Companies are pausing recruitment to better understand the exact skill sets they will require once AI tools are fully integrated.

Capex Bottlenecks and Fed Modernization

  • Infrastructure Capex: Massive capital commitments for data centers and electricity grid demands are causing short-term bottlenecks in sourcing generators and infrastructure equipment.
      • Disinflationary Timeline: While these projects create localized price competition today, they build the physical infrastructure that will ultimately lower operational costs in the long run.
  • San Francisco Housing Supply: Rising regional housing costs and local displacement are driven by a severe lack of housing supply, rather than excessive demand or speculative bubbles.
  • Fed Institutional Leadership: Commenting on the transition to new leadership under Chair Walsh, Daly emphasized the Fed's dual mandate as fiduciary stewards of public trust and public funds.
      • Safe Technology Adoption: The Fed is exploring safe, highly regulated methods to adopt AI in its own operations to increase efficiency without compromising security or human judgment in monetary policymaking.

***

Notable Quotes

"Right now, policy's in a good place. We are prepared to respond either way, whatever the economy brings. But I think giving more forward guidance about what's possible could be misguided in the end because we just have to wait for the economy to evolve." — Mary Daly
"They are interrogating how much AI can do for them before they hire... They want to be patient on their hiring and make sure we're not overhiring." — Mary Daly

Ray Dalio Explains the 5 Forces Shaping the Future of the Global Economy

Forbes(2.1M) ·17:20 ·621 views · 2026-06-04
Ray Dalio (Founder, Bridgewater Associates) Randall Lane (Chief Content Officer, Forbes)
Bridgewater Associates Founder Ray Dalio warns of an impending sovereign debt crisis and a historic shift in global power, advising investors to combat extreme market concentration and geopolitical risk by building portfolios with at least 15 uncorrelated assets.
TL;DR Bridgewater Associates Founder Ray Dalio warns of an impending sovereign debt crisis and a historic shift in global power, advising investors to combat extreme market concentration and geopolitical risk by building portfolios with at least 15 uncorrelated assets. To guide...

TL;DR

Bridgewater Associates Founder Ray Dalio warns of an impending sovereign debt crisis and a historic shift in global power, advising investors to combat extreme market concentration and geopolitical risk by building portfolios with at least 15 uncorrelated assets.

The Five Forces Shaping the Future

To guide global macro investing, Dalio utilizes a historical framework based on five overlapping forces that have driven shifts in wealth and power over the last 500 years:

  • The Debt, Money, and Economic Dynamic: The cycle of credit expansion, asset appreciation, and eventual debt accumulation. When debt growth outpaces income growth, debt servicing costs inevitably squeeze out essential spending.
  • The Internal Political Cycle: The friction between the political left and right, which directly impacts taxation, wealth distribution, and policy stability.
  • Geopolitics: The external shifts in global power and international conflict.
  • Acts of Nature: Historically, pandemics, climate shifts, and natural disasters have killed more people and altered more world orders than wars or economic crises.
  • Technology: Human innovation and invention, spearheaded today by the rapid advancement of artificial intelligence (AI).

Geopolitics: China's Strategy and Changing Global Perceptions

Following a recent trip to China, Dalio highlighted major shifts in global power dynamics and economic strategy:

  • Declining US Deterrence: There is a growing global perception that domestic political divisions and cost-of-living concerns will prevent the US from entering a direct military conflict to defend allies or counter Chinese expansion.
      • Dalio compares this shift to the British Empire's loss of the Suez Canal, which permanently altered the global perception of British military dominance.
  • The Deployment of Chinese Capital: China earned approximately $1.2 trillion from exports last year. How Chinese entities reinvest this massive capital pool globally—particularly in regions like Africa—will heavily impact international capital markets.
  • The "Art of War" Strategy on Taiwan: Rather than a direct military invasion, China is expected to employ gray-zone warfare, leveraging economic pressure, political influence, and strategic deception. This strategy takes advantage of the belief that the US cannot comfortably fight a two-front war.
  • Divergent Views on AI:
      • The US View: Highly commercialized, focused on profitability, capital formation, and dominant market valuations.
      • The Chinese View: Viewed as a public utility like electricity or running water. The goal is to raise overall national productivity rather than secure high corporate profit margins.

The Sovereign Debt "Heart Attack"

Despite the markets ignoring rising debt levels, Dalio maintains that a systemic credit correction is inevitable.

  • The Cardiovascular Analogy: Dalio compares the current global economy to an unhealthy individual with plaque building up in their arteries. Just because they have not suffered a heart attack yet does not mean the underlying behavior is safe.
  • The Yield Curve and Central Banks: The demand for US dollar-denominated debt is weakening.
      • Global central banks are actively selling US dollar debt and purchasing gold.
      • Sustaining these debt levels requires offering higher real returns, which puts upward pressure on the long end of the yield curve.

The Investor Game Plan: The Power of 15 Uncorrelated Bets

In an environment dominated by FOMO (fear of missing out) and extreme market capitalization concentration in tech and AI, Dalio advises investors to prioritize portfolio construction over chasing momentum.

  • Uncorrelated Diversification: The holy grail of investing is holding 15 good, uncorrelated bets.
      • Mathematically, diversifying into 15 independent cash flows can reduce overall portfolio risk by up to 80% without reducing expected returns.
  • Targeting Real Assets: To hedge against monetary inflation and debt-market weakness, investors should look toward global opportunities and physical commodities, such as copper and other critical minerals.

Notable Quotes

"If I can have 15 good uncorrelated bets, what happens—just the math of it—is it means that you can eliminate your risk by up to 80% without reducing your return." — Ray Dalio
"Central banks are selling US dollar-denominated debt and buying gold, and there's a reluctance to accumulate that dollar-denominated debt. That's a reality." — Ray Dalio
"You're either going to deny reality or you can see reality that there is going to be this problem. It’s like telling somebody who has eaten a lot of fat, not exercised, and built a lot of plaque, 'I haven't had a heart attack yet.' ... This is a reality." — Ray Dalio
Please welcome Ray Dalio, founder, Bridgewater Associates, and interviewer, Randall Lane, Chief Content Officer, Forbes. Getting right into it, the big guns. I like it. I like it, I like it. The SpaceX IPO and Data Centers in Space | Stratechery by Ben Thompson

Stratechery(14.9K) ·17:56 ·598 views · 2026-06-04
Ben Thompson (Author, Stratechery)
SpaceX’s highly speculative $2 trillion IPO valuation is driven by Elon Musk's unique ability to convert narrative-driven capital into physical infrastructure, positioning the company to bypass Earth-bound data center zoning and power constraints by ...
TL;DR SpaceX’s highly speculative $2 trillion IPO valuation is driven by Elon Musk's unique ability to convert narrative-driven capital into physical infrastructure, positioning the company to bypass Earth-bound data center zoning and power constraints by deploying...

TL;DR

SpaceX’s highly speculative $2 trillion IPO valuation is driven by Elon Musk's unique ability to convert narrative-driven capital into physical infrastructure, positioning the company to bypass Earth-bound data center zoning and power constraints by deploying laser-interconnected satellite "racks" in space optimized for the massive future market of agentic AI inference.

The Scale Playbook: From Tesla to Starlink

  • Changing the Rules of the Game: Elon Musk's companies succeed not by making cautious, short-term business decisions, but by starting with a massive desired end state and working backward to build unparalleled physical scale.
  • The Starlink Precedent: Starlink has established itself as the dominant player in satellite internet, forcing legacy industries to adapt.
      • Aviation Adoption: Following United Airlines in 2024, American Airlines announced in 2026 that it will install Starlink on over 500 narrowbody aircraft starting in Q1 2027. Multi-gigabit connectivity has shifted from a premium luxury to a customer-retention necessity.
      • Financial Performance: Starlink generated $8.7 billion in revenue and $4.4 billion in profit last year, benefiting heavily from subsidized internal access to SpaceX's dominant launch capacity.

The SpaceX IPO: Reality vs. Meme-Driven Capital

  • Absurd Valuations and Financials: SpaceX is seeking a $2 trillion valuation despite posting $18.67 billion in revenue and $4.9 billion in losses last year, alongside a growth slowdown from 35% to 33%.
      • The AI Drag: The company's massive losses were driven by $5.1 billion in AI R&D expenses associated with xAI and X—funding an AI model currently ranking 5th in the market whose founding team recently departed.
  • A Historically Inflated TAM: The SpaceX S-1 claims an "actionable" Total Addressable Market (TAM) of $28.5 trillion (excluding China and Russia). This includes $26.5 trillion in AI ($22.7 trillion in enterprise applications), which is over 13 times the estimated space and connectivity opportunities combined.
  • The Meme Alchemy of Capital Markets: Just as Tesla avoided bankruptcy by repeatedly issuing stock that retail and meme-driven investors eagerly bought, SpaceX operates on narrative. High-risk stock dilution is viewed by investors as an opportunity to join a movement (e.g., Mars, FSD, AGI), which in turn funds actual, game-changing infrastructure.

The Case for Data Centers in Space

  • The "Satellite-as-a-Rack" Concept: Space-based data centers will not look like terrestrial facilities. Instead, individual disposable satellites will function as single server racks.
      • Form Factor Comparison: The Starlink V2 Mini Direct-to-Cell satellite (estimated at 7.4m x 2.7m x 0.3m) is already physically comparable to Nvidia's NVL72 rack (2.2m x 1.1m x 0.6m).
      • Power and Cooling: While the V2 Mini dissipates 25 kW of energy compared to the NVL72's 135 kW, scaling satellite power closer to 100 kW is feasible. Interconnected satellite constellations using lasers, independent solar panels, and deployable radiator arrays (200+ square meters per rack) can bridge the gap.
  • The Alignment with Agentic Inference: Space data centers are uniquely suited for the impending shift toward "agentic inference" (AI-to-AI workflows).
      • Latency Trade-offs: Unlike real-time human queries, agentic AI runs complex tasks in the background where processing speed is secondary to memory capacity.
      • Hardware Adjustments: Slower, cheaper DRAM memory and larger, less efficient (but more radiation-resistant) chips can be used in space because the systems do not require cutting-edge terrestrial GPU speeds.

Terrestrial Constraints: The "Stick" Driving Space Compute

  • The Power and Zoning Bottleneck: Terrestrial AI expansion is hitting severe roadblocks. While globalization moved factories away without local input, building AI data centers requires local permits.
  • The Rise of Local Opposition: Communities across the U.S. are actively blocking data center construction due to power-grid strain and environmental concerns. Paying communities for permission is an option, but it drastically increases terrestrial costs.
  • Space as the Only Path Forward: As Earth-bound power and political constraints tighten, the infinite space and solar energy of low Earth orbit will transition from an expensive alternative to the only viable scaling option for unlimited compute.

Notable Quotes

"Musk is the master of memes, and is himself a meme. He offers a dream—Mars, fully autonomous vehicles, an addressable market of $28.5 trillion—and positions his companies and their stock as access to that dream. And through the alchemy of capital markets, transforms shared delusion into mass market reality." — Ben Thompson
"What seems very plausible in the long run is that the demand for compute ends up being so large that there eventually is nowhere left to build, making the vast expanses of space not just an alternative, but in fact the only choice." — Ben Thompson
"I am in fact concerned about our ability to muster enough compute to fully realize the gains from AI, and am very worried about a replay of nuclear power, where our failure to build denied us the opportunity to even imagine what could be invented in a world of unlimited energy. The fact Musk is proposing an alternative path to unlimited compute is a relief." — Ben Thompson
The SpaceX IPO and Data Centers in Space was published on Wednesday, May 27, 2026. Listen to this post: It's hardly the biggest problem in the world—or perhaps the height of privilege to consider it a problem at all—but one of the most annoying consumer experiences is booking an Uber Black and realizing you got assigned a Tesla Model Y. Uber finally stopped allowing new Model Ys onto Black last year. Buckle up for an uncomfortable back seat, basic plastic finishes, and all-too-often, potential car sickness from a driver who hasn't completely mastered the Tesla's aggressive regenerative braking. Still, the fact that the Model Y ever made it to the Black level is a testament to the brand Elon Musk built. Back in 2016, when 300,000 people dropped $1,000 each in a matter of hours to reserve an as-yet-unreleased Model 3, I explained that the phenomenon was because "it's a Tesla." "The real payoff of Musk’s Master Plan is the fact that Tesla means something. Yes, it stands for sustainability and caring for the environment. But more important is that Tesla also means amazing performance and Silicon Valley cool. To be sure, Tesla's focus on the high end has helped them move down the cost curve, but it was Musk's insistence on making "an electric car without compromises" that ultimately led to 276,000 people reserving a Model 3, many without even seeing the car. After all, it's a Tesla." End quote. This is the same brand halo that landed what is, if we're honest, a pretty basic car on the Uber Black list. What actually makes these cars compelling is the extent to which they are computers on wheels. I know plenty of very rich people who drive a Tesla not for the finishes, but rather the Full Self-Driving. There is nothing like it on the market, at least when it comes to cars you can own. Tesla appears to be doubling down on this point of differentiation. The company stopped production of the Models S and X earlier this year, focusing production resources on the Cybercab and robots. If you want your car to drive itself, you'll get the same model as everyone else. It reminds me of Andy Warhol's famous quote: "What's great about this country is that America started the tradition where the richest consumers buy essentially the same things as the poorest. You can be watching TV and see Coca-Cola, and you know that the President drinks Coke, Liz Taylor drinks Coke, and just think, you can drink Coke too. A Coke is a Coke, and no amount of money can get you a better Coke than the one the bum on the corner is drinking. All the Cokes are the same, and all the Cokes are good. Liz Taylor knows it, the President knows it, the bum knows it, and you know it." That tradition is scale, and America is indeed better at it than any other country in the world. And amongst Americans, no one pursues and seeks to leverage scale quite like Musk. Starlink and Airlines. From a press release from American Airlines: "American Airlines today announced a sweeping modernization of its narrowbody inflight customer experience with the installation of Starlink, the fastest Wi-Fi in the sky, on more than 500 narrowbody aircraft beginning in Q1 2027. Starlink is widely regarded as the world's most advanced satellite constellation using a low Earth orbit to deliver broadband Internet capable of supporting inflight streaming, online gaming, collaborative meeting tools, and more. With thousands of satellites in low Earth orbit, Starlink can deliver multi-gigabit connectivity to aircraft using its Aero Terminal, which can support up to 1 gigabit per second per antenna. "As a premium global airline, we are continuously seeking out world-class partners like Starlink to deliver what our customers need and want," said American Airlines Chief Customer Officer Heather Garboden. "The addition of Starlink solidifies American as a leading airline in keeping passengers connected in flight." As part of American's commitment to an elevated onboard experience, Starlink will enable seamless streaming, browsing, and real-time communication capabilities across American's domestic and short-haul international routes." I linked to the press release just for the amusement of American Airlines, which has in recent years built its strategy around offering anything but premium on routes you need, billing their Starlink deal as a commitment to quote "an elevated onboard experience" end quote. That may have been the argument for United's Starlink deal when it was announced in 2024, but by this point it's tablestakes, which is surely exactly how Musk wants it. Starlink is the consumer-facing business of SpaceX, generating $8.7 billion in revenue last year and $4.4 billion in profit. While it's not totally clear exactly how SpaceX accounts for launch costs, obviously Starlink benefits greatly from the fact that it has access to SpaceX's launch capacity. That launch capacity has resulted in over ten thousand active satellites in low Earth orbit, delivering low latency high speed Internet anywhere in the world, including in the air. That's the carrot for airlines. The stick is the prospect of everyone else having the same service, and customers making flight decisions based on the quality of Internet access available. There is a similarity to Tesla in this way. Musk companies at their best don't win the game, they change the rules through scale, such that billionaires buy economy cars because they actually drive themselves. And airlines transform the consumer experience on their own dime. Musk makes all-in bets, whether that be in terms of launch capacity or in autonomous driving, not by making rational short-term business decisions, but by starting with the desired end state and working backwards. SpaceX's Silly S-1. Tech has a long history of silly charts. There is an entire category known as Bezos Charts. And the SpaceX S-1 has one that made me laugh. It came in discussion of SpaceX's total addressable market. "We believe we have identified the largest actionable total addressable market, TAM, in human history. We estimate that our quantifiable TAM is $28.5 trillion, consisting of $370 billion in Space from space-enabled solutions, $1.6 trillion in Connectivity across $870 billion in Starlink Broadband and $740 billion in Starlink Mobile, as well as additional opportunities in enterprise and government. This TAM also includes $26.5 trillion in AI across $2.4 trillion in AI infrastructure, $760 billion in consumer subscriptions, $600 billion in digital advertising, and $22.7 trillion in enterprise applications. For illustrative purposes of sizing our addressable market opportunity, we exclude China and Russia from our global estimates." This image is approximately to scale vertically, but certainly not horizontally. I could use the help in really wrapping my mind around the $26.5 trillion AI opportunity, given it's more than 13 times the space and connectivity opportunity combined. In all seriousness, the numbers are obviously absurd. But then again, everything about this IPO is absurd. SpaceX is seeking a $2 trillion valuation on a mere $18.67 billion in revenue, with $4.9 billion in losses last year, and growth actually slowed from 35% to 33%. That slowdown happened despite the addition of xAI, and thus also X, which tipped the company from a small profit to that massive loss, thanks to $5.1 billion in AI R&D expense. That R&D, keep in mind, went towards building a model that is in 5th place, and whose entire founding team recently left the company. But sure, $26.5 trillion AI opportunity. This is not to say that SpaceX won't get its desired valuation. Tesla's valuation never made any sense right up until the Models 3 and Y actually worked out, causing Tesla's share price to soar. And even then it was hard to ever build a financial model that justified the new share price. Musk's ability to make his own reality starts with investors. From 2021's Mistakes and Memes and comparing Apple and Tesla: "The comparison works as far as it goes, but it doesn't tell the entire story. After all, Apple's brand was derived from decades building products, which had made it the most profitable company in the world. Tesla, meanwhile, always seemed to be weeks from going bankrupt, at least until it issued ever more stock, strengthening the conviction of Tesla skeptics and shorts. That, though, was the crazy thing. You would think that issuing stock would lead to Tesla's stock price slumping. After all, existing shares were being diluted. Time after time, though, Tesla's announcements about stock issuances would lead to the stock going up. It didn't make any sense, at least if you thought about the stock as representing a company. It turned out, though, that TSLA was a meme, one about a car company, but also sustainability, and most of all, about Elon Musk himself. Issuing more stock was not diluting existing shareholders, it was extending the opportunity to propagate the TSLA meme to that many more people. And while Musk's haters multiplied, so did his fans. The Internet, after all, is about abundance, not scarcity. The end result is that instead of infrastructure leading to a movement, a movement via the stock market funded the buildout of infrastructure." End quote. I explained in that Article why I generally did not cover Tesla's financial results, and the reasoning extends to why I don't expect to cover SpaceX's. Musk is the master of memes, and is himself a meme. He offers a dream—Mars, fully autonomous vehicles, an addressable market of $28.5 trillion—and positions his companies and their stock as access to that dream. And through the alchemy of capital markets, he transforms shared delusion into mass market reality. Musk’s track record matters in this regard. Building an electric car company was possible, as was Full Self-Driving. At the same time, there were ever increasing government mandates and programs around decreasing emissions, that acted as the stick to Tesla's carrot. Similarly, landing rockets was possible, and the new market creation downstream from correspondingly lower launch costs was comprehensible. That Musk succeeded in both instances gives him the benefit of the doubt. The question that matters, then, is not if the numbers make sense right now. They absolutely do not. What matters is if the dream is even possible, and if there are actual reasons to think it might happen. I think that data centers in space meet these conditions. The Case for Data Centers in Space. The first question about data centers in space is if they are even possible, and I think the answer is clearly yes. The key thing to consider is that there is no requirement that these data centers look anything like data centers on earth. On earth we build massive buildings full of GPUs with massive infrastructure for cooling those GPUs, and massive power plants, or connection to a grid which connects to massive power plants, to power those GPUs. The idea of transporting these massive structures to space sounds implausible, and it is. However, there is no reason that space data centers would look like data centers on earth. What makes far more sense is to think about an individual satellite as something akin to a rack. Right now, the largest Starlink satellite in orbit is the V2 Mini Direct-to-Cell, which measures 7.4 meters by 2.7 meters by 0.3 meters, estimated. An NVL72 rack from Nvidia, meanwhile, measures 2.2 meters by 1.1 meters by 0.6 meters, so we're already in the right size range. The V2 Mini Direct-to-Cell consumes and dissipates up to an estimated 25 kilowatts of energy, the NVL72 up to 135 kilowatts, and it can fit a 1 trillion parameter model quantized to FP4. The big shortcoming for a rack satellite is power and its dissipation, but going from 25 kilowatts to 135 kilowatts is certainly within the realm of possibility. Given that you don't need much of the cooling and power distribution usage on earth, something closer to 100 kilowatts might deliver similar performance. There are other issues to address, including the problem of radiation screwing with calculations, reliability, etc., although those two concerns could be addressed in part by using larger chips, which are less efficient, but also use less power. These rack satellites will also be disposable, like Starlink satellites, ameliorating reliability issues. The key factor, however, is that a fleet of racks interconnected with lasers, as Starlink's already are, each with their own solar panels and radiator arrays for cooling (deploying 200+ square meters of radiators per rack will be a huge challenge), is possible. The next question about data centers in space is if there is a use case for them—the carrot—and I already made the argument that there is in "The Inference Shift". Specifically, there are three types of workloads developing around LLMs: training, answer inference, and agentic inference. From the section making the case for agentic inference: "Critically, this articulation of an agentic-specific memory hierarchy implies a necessary trade-off of speed for capacity. Here's the thing, though. Lower speed isn't nearly as important a consideration if there isn't a human in the loop. If an agent is waiting around for a job that is being run overnight, the agent doesn't know or care about the user experience impact. What is most important is being able to accomplish a task, and if entirely new approaches to memory make that possible, then delays are fine. If delays are fine, then all of the focus on pure compute power and high-bandwidth memory seems out of place. If latency isn't the top priority, then slower and cheaper memory, like traditional DRAM, for example, makes a lot more sense. And if the entire system is mostly waiting on memory, then chips don't need to be as fast as the cutting edge either. This represents a profound shift in future architectures, but it also doesn't mean that current architectures are going away. Training will continue to matter, and Nvidia's current architecture, including high speed compute, large amounts of high bandwidth memory, and high speed networking, will likely continue to dominate. Answer inference will be a meaningful market, albeit a relatively small one, and speed from chips like Cerebras or Groq (I explained how Nvidia is deploying Groq's LPUs here) will be very useful. Agentic inference will gradually unbundle the GPU, which alternates between stranding high-bandwidth memory during the prefill process, and stranding compute during the decode process, in favor of increasingly sophisticated memory hierarchies, dominated by high capacity and relatively lower cost memory types, with good enough compute. Indeed, if anything, it will be the speed of CPUs for things like tool use that will matter more than the speed of GPUs. At the same time, these categories won't be equal in size or importance. Specifically, agentic inference will be the largest market by far, because that is the market that won't be limited by humans or time. Today's agents are fancy answer inference. In the future, true agentic inference will be work done by computers according to dictates given by other computers, and the market size scales not with humans, but with compute." End quote. It's agentic inference that makes the most sense for racks in space, and conveniently enough, that is also the market that is likely to be the largest in the long run. The third question about data centers in space is if there is a stick. Specifically, while I think that racks-in-space are both a lot more viable than people think, and a lot more relevant to agentic inference than current modes of compute, it is at the end of the day cheaper and easier to build on earth, all things being equal. All things are not equal, however. Right now we are at the very beginning of the AI buildout, and already one of the biggest constraints is not just power, expected, but zoning, unexpected. I wrote in an update last week: "That leads to an interesting contrast to globalization. When companies were closing down American factories and laying off workers and moving operations to China, none of the affected towns or workers had a say. They just suddenly no longer had a job, and a huge number of cities across the Rust Belt no longer had a reason to exist. People simply had to move, or worse, retreat to things like alcohol or drugs. AI, however, is the opposite. Building data centers requires permission, which is to say that people actually have a say. Again, I'm not at all saying that these people are well-informed about data centers, or about the economic impact on their communities, much less the economic impact of AI generally. What I am noting is that people who didn't have a say in globalization are suddenly finding they do have a say about AI, and it's not a surprise they are expressing their disapproval by blocking data centers." End quote. In that Update, I made the case that data center builders, and by extension the companies that use them, should straight up pay people for permission to build data centers in their communities. At a minimum, however, that increases the costs of terrestrial data centers. What seems very plausible in the long run is that the demand for compute ends up being so large that there eventually is nowhere left to build, making the vast expanses of space not just an alternative, but in fact the only choice. An IPO Worth Supporting. If all of this happens, and there are a lot of ifs here, then suddenly that $2 trillion valuation starts looking reasonable. SpaceX is already monetizing xAI's first data center, Colossus 1, to the tune of $15 billion a year for 300 megawatts of capacity. That's 3,000 racks-in-space. Anthropic, meanwhile, will probably make 3x the revenue on that capacity. It remains to be seen if xAI can get back in the state-of-the-art game, but if so, then the amount of revenue it can generate per rack-in-space will be commensurately higher. Even without xAI, however, SpaceX has the potential to be a monopoly provider of marginal compute capacity. There are, needless to say, a massive number of assumptions baked into this argument, including assuming a huge number of engineering challenges are solved, Starship actually works, SpaceX gets sufficient supply of the right kind of chips, compute demand is massively larger, agentic inference unbundles current architectures, and data center opponents are successful. The risk attached to all these assumptions should discount the valuation you put on this business, which is to say I still think this IPO is nuts. At the same time, I'm glad it exists for multiple reasons. The first one is the most obvious one. Musk, for all of his faults, has already pushed humanity forward on multiple vectors, including electric cars, self-driving, reusable rockets, satellite internet, etc., and I'm excited to see him try and do more. The second is that I am in fact concerned about our ability to muster enough compute to fully realize the gains from AI, and am very worried about a replay of nuclear power, where our failure to build denied us the opportunity to even imagine what could be invented in a world of unlimited energy. The fact Musk is proposing an alternative path to unlimited compute is a relief. The third is that I appreciate the extent to which this IPO is a return to what an IPO should be: the opportunity for people to contribute capital to actually build the business, and to benefit if it works out. As I noted, I can't make a financial model that necessarily justifies this valuation, particularly based on current financials, but neither can a VC investing in the Series A of a company. SpaceX has already invented a lot, and its early investors are going to make a lot of money with this IPO. At the same time, there is still so much more to invent that there remains a lot of upside, and to be very clear, a lot of risk. It's a testament to SpaceX's ambitions that retail investors get to play VC. And hey, you get Mars upside for free.

Verizon CEO on Using Tech to Transform Telecom

Bloomberg Live(185.0K) ·21:26 ·128 views · 2026-06-04
Dan Schulman (CEO, Verizon) Tom Giles (Bloomberg)
Verizon CEO Dan Schulman is driving an aggressive cultural and technological turnaround to reverse five years of market share loss, prioritizing customer experience improvements and leveraging advanced AI agents that have already boosted customer satisfaction by 1,280 basis points.
TL;DR Verizon CEO Dan Schulman is driving an aggressive cultural and technological turnaround to reverse five years of market share loss, prioritizing customer experience improvements and leveraging advanced AI agents that have already boosted customer satisfaction by 1,280...

TL;DR

Verizon CEO Dan Schulman is driving an aggressive cultural and technological turnaround to reverse five years of market share loss, prioritizing customer experience improvements and leveraging advanced AI agents that have already boosted customer satisfaction by 1,280 basis points.

Cultural Turnaround and Strategic Pivot

  • Reversing Market Decline: After taking over as CEO, Schulman addressed Verizon's five-year consecutive loss in market share, which had dragged the company from first to last in both market cap and forward P/E.
  • Increasing Risk Tolerance: Schulman is shifting the corporate culture from a bureaucratic "two or three" risk tolerance to an "eight or nine" to encourage rapid experimentation and failing fast.
      • *Exceptions:* Risk tolerance remains at a strict "one" for cybersecurity and financial reporting integrity.
  • Value Over Price Wars: Verizon is rejecting short-term promotional price wars, which Schulman notes can be matched by competitors within two hours, in favor of building unique, long-term value propositions.

Customer Experience and Churn Mitigation

  • Halting Arbitrary Price Hikes: To combat rising customer churn, Schulman instituted a rule to "stop digging," halting Verizon's previous practice of implementing price increases (30 over three years) without delivering incremental customer value.
  • Eliminating Friction Points: The company is focusing on solving retail and billing pain points, ensuring that in-store promises accurately reflect on a customer's first bill and that customer service agents are highly empathetic.
  • Continuous Incremental Improvement: Instead of seeking a singular "big bang" product launch, Verizon is focusing on daily, steady improvements in loyalty, plan simplicity, and tailored personalization.

AI-Led Transformation and Workforce Impact

  • Stellar AI Agent Performance: In pilot testing over a three-month period, Verizon's conversational AI customer service agents achieved customer satisfaction rates 1,280 basis points higher than previous baselines.
  • Job Displacement Realities: Schulman acknowledges that AI will likely displace a "large percentage" of rote customer service jobs (e.g., password resets and billing inquiries), though complex issues will shift to collaborative human-machine workflows.
      • This follows an earlier, difficult corporate restructuring that eliminated 13,000 jobs at Verizon.
  • Reskilling and Funding: Verizon has launched a $20 million training and reskilling initiative for employees and communities impacted by AI, with over 7,000 applicants already engaged. Schulman is advocating in Washington D.C. for a broader, coordinated Fortune 500 and government-funded reskilling coalition.
  • Rapid Tech Timelines: Schulman predicts the industry will achieve Artificial General Intelligence (AGI) within two to four years, humanoid robotics shortly thereafter, and is currently preparing Verizon for Quantum decryption risks ("Q-Day") expected as early as two years from now.

Advanced Cybersecurity and Network Autonomy

  • Deploying Mythos: Partnering with Glasswing, Verizon is utilizing "Mythos," an AI-driven cybersecurity tool that aggregates minor network vulnerabilities to identify and preemptively patch critical systemic risks.
  • Autonomous Network Healing: Within the next few months, Verizon aims to autonomously identify and patch 85% of network vulnerabilities within minutes, a process that previously took hours or days of manual labor.

Notable Quotes

"Anytime the pace of change outside is greater than the pace of change inside, we're falling behind, and that's a really high bar to get over." — Dan Schulman
"The first rule of getting out of a hole is stop digging." — Dan Schulman
"You should not be scared of AI. You need to learn AI. You need to be able to use it in your work. You need to be able to use it in your personal life because that's the age we live in." — Dan Schulman
