Anthropic President Amodei on the Future of Claude

Bloomberg Technology(727.0K) ·23:28 ·2.1K views · 2026-06-04
Daniela Amodei (President & Co-Founder, Anthropic) Shirin Ghaffary (Bloomberg)
Anthropic President Daniela Amodei highlights the company's rapid scaling—marked by $7.3 billion raised and a projected $850 million annualized revenue run rate—while outlining how strategic, multi-cloud partnerships with Amazon and Google fuel capit...
TL;DR Anthropic President Daniela Amodei highlights the company's rapid scaling—marked by $7.3 billion raised and a projected $850 million annualized revenue run rate—while outlining how strategic, multi-cloud partnerships with Amazon and Google fuel capital-intensive frontier...

TL;DR

Anthropic President Daniela Amodei highlights the company's rapid scaling—marked by $7.3 billion raised and a projected $850 million annualized revenue run rate—while outlining how strategic, multi-cloud partnerships with Amazon and Google fuel capital-intensive frontier training without compromising the startup's independent, safety-first mission.

Leadership Dynamics and Scaling Humility

  • Divergent Co-Founder Roles: Daniela Amodei and her brother, CEO Dario Amodei, split duties to balance long-term technical ambition with operational execution.
      • Dario Amodei (CEO): Acts as the technical visionary, directing model scale, capabilities, and future research directions.
      • Daniela Amodei (President): Manages the day-to-day organization, leads the executive team, and drives decisions regarding customers, product strategy, and business applications.
  • Conflict Resolution: Relying on decades of shared history, the co-founders resolve business disagreements by prioritizing curiosity over division. Approximately 80% to 90% of disagreements end in a hybrid "third way" compromise rather than a binary win-lose outcome.
  • Rejecting "Frontrunner" Distractions: Despite rapid commercial success, Amodei emphasizes that chasing metrics or competitor rankings is the wrong focus. The company's primary objective remains building ethical, responsible, and fair AI technologies.

Funding, Capital Intensity, and Tech Giant Partnerships

  • Capital Mobilization: Anthropic has raised $7.3 billion in funding to date and is projecting an annualized revenue run rate of $850 million, highlighting the massive capital requirements of the sector.
  • Strategic Mega-Deals: Anthropic recently secured a $4 billion partnership with Amazon and a prior $2 billion deal with Google.
      • Distribution and Reach: Rather than just acquiring compute resources, these partnerships allow Anthropic to deploy Claude directly within the existing cloud environments (Amazon Bedrock and Google Vertex AI) where enterprise data already resides.
  • Preserving Autonomy: Despite deep financial and infrastructure ties to massive tech partners, Anthropic remains an independent company, maintaining total control over its product roadmap, research directions, and corporate governance.

Enterprise Traction and Multi-Cloud Strategy

  • Operational Resilience via Multi-Cloud: Anthropic runs its model training and inference workloads across both AWS and Google Cloud. This internal multi-cloud setup provides operational redundancy and deep insight into how customers experience these distinct environments.
  • Enterprise Growth Drivers: Scale is heavily driven by large enterprises in the financial services, legal, healthcare, and technology sectors.
      • The 200k Context Advantage: Corporate clients leverage Claude's 200k token context window to process massive, multi-document workloads, such as auditing entire codebases, legal documents, and dozens of financial reports simultaneously.
  • Preventing Vendor Lock-In: Amodei supports a "multi-model, multi-cloud" future where enterprises deploy diverse AI models tailored to specific use cases rather than relying on a single, winner-take-all provider.

Constitutional AI and the Safety-Frontier Alignment

  • Constitutional AI vs. RLHF: Unlike traditional Reinforcement Learning from Human Feedback (RLHF)—which can suffer from scalability limits and models trying to superficially please human raters—Anthropic uses Constitutional AI.
      • Principles-Based Training: Models are trained using a written "constitution" (incorporating sources like the UN Declaration of Human Rights). The model is programmed to critique its own outputs against these principles before human-reinforced training is applied.
      • Transparency: This approach provides a clear, inspectable set of values, building greater trust with enterprise clients and regulators.
  • The Frontier Safety Imperative: Anthropic maintains that a company must build state-of-the-art frontier models to truly understand and mitigate advanced AI risks. Safety research cannot be executed effectively in a vacuum or on lagging technology.

Notable Quotes

"I have to say like, I don't know how one CEO just runs a company by themselves. It seems like it's actually much easier to do with two people, particularly when they know each other really well." — Daniela Amodei
"It's a very capital-intensive business to train AI models... my guess is that over time, the sort of core set of companies that are working to advance the frontier are just going to need access to capital. And I think the cloud partnership is very well suited to that." — Daniela Amodei
"We believe that the only way to build safe models is to be at the frontier of building these models, because you have to understand what these models are capable of in order to understand how to make them safe. If you are not at the frontier, you don't actually know what the risks are." — Daniela Amodei
We were chatting not so long ago about when we first met and started covering Anthropic. But for those who aren't familiar, you and your brother, Dario, and a handful of other people left OpenAI to start Anthropic back in 2021. Now you employ thousands of employees, many of them in a reporting chain up to you in your role as President. I'm curious, what is the difference in the leadership style between you and Dario? Well, first of all, thank you so much for having me. It's great to be here today. And, you know, Dario and I, I really think of how we sort of split duties. Dario is this incredible technical visionary. I think, you know, years before AI was this amazing idea that people were excited about around the world, he had this really strong conviction that artificial intelligence is going to be a big deal. This is like the early 2010s. And I think Dario is constantly reminding the company about the scale, the ambition, and sort of where the technology itself is going. I think of my role much more as actually running and managing the day-to-day of the organization. So, I manage the executive team, and we spend time making decisions about customers, about our products, about the research, and how that applies to the people that are using artificial intelligence. And I like to think that the two of us are a great balance. I have to say, like, I don't know how one CEO just runs a company by themselves. It seems like it's actually much easier to do with two people, particularly when they know each other really well. I remember, in one of our conversations earlier, you telling me that you and Dario rarely fight. And I'm curious how you manage. What are some examples of places where maybe you have disagreed and have been able to settle it in a healthy way with regard to the business? Well, as someone who also has a brother, I know you know. I think we've had several decades to kind of practice fighting and still loving each other, right? So, as early as like, "Why did you take my toy? Can I have that back, please?" I think that has served us well in being able to operate well together at Anthropic. I think what I would say is, you know, we do disagree sometimes. But I think how you disagree really matters. And I feel like there is always an incredible mutual respect between us. So, if we look at the same situation and we see something different, I like to think that what our default is is to be curious, right? So, I usually view it as like, "Oh, this is not like an obvious call to me. Why do you see it that way?" Because usually, he'll have a piece of information that I don't have, or he'll have a different perspective. And I would say about 80 to 90% of the time, what ends up happening is we meet in the middle. And so, we'll think like, "Oh, I think this is the right thing to do, you think this is the right thing to do." Actually, there's probably a third way to do this that's the combination of what we both think, which is actually a little bit better. I think that's part of the strength of having both of us—the ability to practice looking at situations and decisions just from different points of view and perspective, and I think it helps us make better decisions. So, two years ago when you were sitting on the Bloomberg Tech stage with Dario, Anthropic was still very much the underdog in the competition against OpenAI and just in the race to AI generally. Now it's in a very different position, no longer the underdog. Claude is a runaway success, particularly among coders, your company is projecting 850 million dollars in annualized run rate revenue, and the company has raised 7.3 billion dollars in funding to date. Do you feel like you're definitively the frontrunner right now? And how does that change your approach? You know, something that I think both Dario and I really hammer at Anthropic is that that is just completely the wrong way to think. I think our view has always been, you know, this is really about how do we show up every day for our customers, how do we stay humble and make sure that we're really focused on the mission? Right, the whole reason we started Anthropic is we want to be able to build and develop this technology in a way that is ethical, that's responsible, that's fair. And I think it's really incumbent upon everybody at the company, but especially leadership, to say, "You know, all of these numbers, they're actually not the point, right? We are really here to do a job. We're here to support the businesses that rely on us every day." AI is increasingly a bigger part of everybody's workflow, their experience at the office, their experience in their personal lives. That's a huge privilege and responsibility. And so, I think if anything, we work really hard every day to say like, "Our job is to be the best version of who we can possibly be as a company every day." That takes work, that takes humility, that takes time, that takes focus and energy. I want to talk about the big news earlier this week that Anthropic has partnered with Amazon on a 4 billion dollar deal. And before that, of course, a 2 billion dollar deal with Google. How do you manage those relationships, especially as you also compete with them in some areas? And do you think that there's a risk of being too dependent on these tech giants? So, yes, we're really proud of, you know, both of those partnerships. And, you know, I think they give us the sort of resources to potentially train these very, very large models. But I think, you know, more importantly, they are really about how we reach the customers that already use these cloud providers. Right, so Amazon and Google, they have massive enterprise footprints. And for us, being able to make Claude available to those customers in the environments where they already have their data, where they're already running their workloads, is just incredibly valuable. And so, I think we've been able to maintain our independence. Right, we are still an independent company, we make our own decisions about our product roadmap, about our research. But we also have these very deep, strategic partnerships with two of the most important tech companies in the world, and I think that's actually been a really, really great balance for us. I mean, if we can just maybe level up a little, what is the calculus for an AI company generally to partner with a tech giant? What are some of the pros and cons here? Because on the one hand, yes, you get the resources and the distribution, but on the other, then you're beholden to their interests, maybe their cloud infrastructure, etc. So, what are some of the pros and cons, just generally, for an AI startup, do you think, to partner with a tech giant in this moment? So, I think, at least speaking for ourselves, and I think, ideally, probably really for the AI industry more broadly, it's a very capital-intensive business to train AI models. I think that's something we've been, you know, very open about from day one, and I don't think that's a surprise in the industry. And I think this kind of concept of like, how do you access the level of capital that you need to train these models, right? It's a really big kind of upfront cost to train the models and to serve inference on them. And I think that is, you know, my guess is that over time, the sort of core set of companies that are working to advance the frontier are just going to need access to capital. And I think the cloud partnership is very well suited to that. So, there are obviously of course tradeoffs, but my sense is there's just a fundamental structure of how training and kind of serving these models for customers works, that it will require that level of access. It's clear that many businesses can't get enough of Claude. But at the same time, we are hearing about some customers who are concerned about the pitfalls of so-called vendor lock-in, of, you know, being too dependent on a single provider. Google's cloud head, Thomas Kurian, recently talked about how they are seeing a lot of interest in multi-cloud strategies. Do you think there's truth to the idea that some businesses are looking to diversify their AI partnerships, and how does Anthropic fit into a multi-cloud world? So, I think, I think kind of two, two things are true at once here. I think, you know, first of all, our approach from day one has been to make Claude available on as many platforms as possible. Right, so we are on Amazon Bedrock, we are on Google Vertex, we have our own API, and we are on other platforms as well. And I think that's because we believe that customers should have choice. Right, they should be able to run these models in the environment that works best for them, whether that's because of security or latency or just because that's where their other cloud workloads are. And I think the second thing that's true is, we do see a lot of customers who want to use multiple models, right? So they might use Claude for some tasks, they might use other models for other tasks. And I think that's a very healthy way for the ecosystem to develop. It means that there isn't just one winner, but that there's a really diverse set of models that are suited to different use cases. And we're really comfortable in that world because we feel like Claude's unique strengths, especially around, you know, reasoning, around long context, around safety, really make it stand out. Does Anthropic have a multi-cloud strategy itself? We do, yeah. So we run on both AWS and Google Cloud. And we use both of those for our training, for our inference. And I think that's been really valuable for us as a company because it gives us that operational resilience, and it also means that we can see, you know, how these different clouds work from the inside, which helps us build better products for our customers who are also running in those environments. So, we're very much a multi-cloud company ourselves. What type of customer is driving the most growth for Claude right now? Is it the large enterprises, the startups, or individual developers? Honestly, it's a mix of all three, but I think, you know, enterprise is really where we are seeing some of the most exciting scale right now. We have companies in financial services, in legal, in healthcare, in tech, who are taking Claude and really integrating it deep into their workflows. And that's because, you know, Claude's long-context window, our 200k context window, allows them to upload entire codebases or, you know, dozens of financial reports or legal documents, and have Claude analyze them all at once. And that's a use case that is just incredibly valuable for large organizations. But at the same time, we're also seeing great traction with startups who are building new applications on top of Claude, and with developers who are using it for coding and other tasks. So, it's really a very broad-based growth. Dario and Anthropic's leadership has been vocal about the need for safety, and you've pioneered this concept of Constitutional AI. Can you tell us a bit about that? How is it different from traditional RLHF, and why do you think it's a better approach? Yeah, so Constitutional AI is really our approach to training models to be helpful, honest, and harmless, using a set of principles, like a constitution, rather than relying solely on human feedback. With traditional RLHF, which is Reinforcement Learning from Human Feedback, you have humans constantly rating model outputs, which is, first of all, very hard to scale, but also can sometimes lead to models just trying to please the human rather than being truly helpful or honest. With Constitutional AI, we give the model a written set of principles, things like the UN Declaration of Human Rights or, you know, principles about being helpful and objective. And we train the model to critique its own outputs based on those principles, and then we use RL to train the final model. And I think the reason we like this approach is, first of all, it's much more scalable, but more importantly, it's much more transparent. Right, anyone can look at the constitution we used and see exactly what values the model was trained on, which we think is incredibly important for trust. You've also been vocal about the potential risks of AI, and Dario signed the statement on AI risk last year alongside many other leaders. How do you balance that, you know, that dual mission of wanting to build these incredibly powerful tools but also being deeply concerned about the risks they pose? Yeah, I think it's, you know, it is a very difficult balance, but I actually think the two parts of the mission are deeply connected. Right, so we believe that the only way to build safe models is to be at the frontier of building these models, because you have to understand, you know, what these models are capable of in order to understand how to make them safe. If you are not at the frontier, you don't actually know what the risks are. And so, we feel like our role is to be a safety-focused lab that is also at the frontier of capabilities. And we hope that by doing that, we can show the rest of the industry that it's possible to build, you know, very powerful, state-of-the-art models in a way that is responsible and safe. And we also hope that we can help set, you know, standards and benchmarks for safety that the rest of the industry can adopt. So, we really see the two parts as, as reinforcing each other. And what's next for Claude? What can we expect from Anthropic in the coming months? Is there a Claude 3 on the horizon? Well, we're always working on the next generation of models, but I can't say anything specific about, you know, release dates or version names. But I think what you can expect is, we're going to continue to focus on, on those core strengths, so making the models better at reasoning, making them more reliable, making them safer, and also continuing to build out the features that our enterprise customers need. Things like security, administrative controls, and integrations with their existing tools. So, we have a very busy roadmap, and we're really excited about what's coming. Well, Daniela, thank you so much for joining us. It's been great having you. Thank you so much. Great to be here.

Is the Global Economy at a Turning Point? Carlyle CEO Harvey Schwartz Explains

Forbes(2.1M) ·14:02 ·165 views · 2026-06-04
Harvey Schwartz (CEO, Carlyle) Steve Forbes (Chairman & Editor-in-Chief, Forbes)
The global economy is undergoing a historic inflection point as 80 years of post-WWII globalization and geopolitical cooperation reverse in favor of national security, onshoring, and energy diversification.
TL;DR The global economy is undergoing a historic inflection point as 80 years of post-WWII globalization and geopolitical cooperation reverse in favor of national security, onshoring, and energy diversification. While geopolitical tail risks remain difficult for markets to...

TL;DR

The global economy is undergoing a historic inflection point as 80 years of post-WWII globalization and geopolitical cooperation reverse in favor of national security, onshoring, and energy diversification. While geopolitical tail risks remain difficult for markets to price and private credit faces structural adjustments, the underlying financial system remains robust and presents vast opportunities for private capital.

The Rewiring of the Global Economic Model

  • Reversal of Multi-Decade Trends: The fundamental trends that defined the global economy for the past 80 years—globalization, post-WWII geopolitical cooperation, and frictionless mobility of capital and labor—are slowing down or reversing, with technology being the sole exception that continues to accelerate.
  • The Shift to Energy Diversification: Global discussions have pivoted from "energy transition" to "energy diversification" because energy security is now treated strictly as an issue of national security.
  • Onshoring and Deglobalization:
      • Supply Chain Resilience: The 1990s focus on "just-in-time" inventory and global supply chain efficiency is being replaced by onshoring and localized economic growth to eliminate international dependency.
      • The Cost of Rebuilding: While geopolitical cooperation can be dismantled quickly, physically unwinding and rebuilding complex global supply chains is a capital-intensive, multi-year process.
  • The Demand for Private Capital: Ballooning government deficits mean public funds cannot support this global transition. The trillions of dollars required for energy diversification and onshoring over the next 10 to 15 years must come from private sources, including banks, private equity, and private credit.

Regional Growth and Market Dynamics

  • Japan's Structural Pivot: After 30 years of stagnation and deflation, Japan is actively executing a multi-pillared growth strategy.
      • Capital Activation: The government is encouraging citizens to move wealth out of bank savings accounts and into investable assets, alongside reforming corporate behaviors and investing in national defense.
      • Investor Sentiment: Sentiment toward Japan has turned highly positive as capital moves freely to chase these structural reforms.
  • Carlyle Portfolio Performance: Carlyle manages approximately $500 billion in assets, with portfolio companies employing 750,000 people globally.
      • Key Indicators: Portfolios show solid EBITDA growth and stable (though slowing) hiring.
      • The AI Spending Squeeze: Heavy investments in IT and AI implementation are currently squeezing out other capital allocation options within businesses.

Pricing Risks: Geopolitics vs. Financial Systems

  • The Difficulty of Pricing Geopolitical Risk: Geopolitical tail risk is nearly impossible for markets to price accurately, leading to stark pricing anomalies.
      • Disproportionate Reactions: The collapse of Silicon Valley Bank moved financial markets roughly 10 times more than Russia’s invasion of Ukraine. This demonstrates that markets over-respond to immediate financial system shocks while struggling to price long-term geopolitical shifts.
  • A Robust Financial System: Unlike the pre-2008 era, the post-crisis regulatory work done over the last decade has left the core global banking and financial systems highly resilient.

The Reality of Private Credit and Systemic Risk

  • An Essential Capital Source: Since the year 2000, the number of public companies globally has decreased by half. Private credit has stepped in to provide the essential capital required for private middle-market companies to scale.
  • Correction of the 2021–2022 Bubble: During 2021 and 2022, a bubble formed in private credit as assets were acquired at highly inflated multiples.
      • While companies with weak business models and poor capital structures will struggle, high-quality businesses with challenged balance sheets are expected to successfully navigate the transition.
  • No Impending Systemic Domino Effect: The private credit market does not pose a systemic risk to the global economy.
      • Lack of Crisis Triggers: A systemic crisis requires a combination of excessive leverage, high liquidity, deep interconnectedness, and panic—variables that do not align in the current private credit landscape.

Notable Quotes

"I think we're actually in the early stages of the most significant inflection point in recent financial history." — Harvey Schwartz
"If we were sitting here at this conference in the '90s, we would all be talking about just-in-time inventory and the need to be globally competitive... You can unwind geopolitical cooperation very quickly, it's much harder to unwind and redo all that supply chain work." — Harvey Schwartz
"The one thing about systemic risk is you need a handful of variables. You need lots of leverage, lots of liquidity, lots of interconnected activity, and you need panic... [Private credit] doesn't really pass the test." — Harvey Schwartz

TL;DR True business value in the AI era is unlocked not by generic large language models, but by combining LLMs with Palantir’s Ontology and Apollo deployment infrastructure. This integration allows organizations to digitally encode their unique, proprietary "tradecraft" to...

TL;DR

True business value in the AI era is unlocked not by generic large language models, but by combining LLMs with Palantir’s Ontology and Apollo deployment infrastructure. This integration allows organizations to digitally encode their unique, proprietary "tradecraft" to automate decisions, orchestrate complex physical and digital assets, and protect critical operations at machine speed.

Bridging AI with Operational Reality: The Power of the Ontology

  • The Fallacy of Generic LLMs: Palantir CEO Dr. Alex Karp warned that off-the-shelf public models treat businesses as generic entities, which can lead to rapid token expenditures without delivering actual competitive advantages.
    • Karp emphasized that businesses must own their "means of production" and "alpha-generating data," rather than exporting proprietary intellectual property to public clouds.
  • The "Tradecraft" Advantage: Real value comes from digitally encoding the tribal knowledge, edge cases, and judgment of human domain experts into the software system.
    • The Ontology Concept: Palantir’s ontology serves as the centralized, semantic map of an enterprise, translating fragmented datasets into real-world concepts (e.g., a specific vehicle, a client contract, or a farmer's plot of land).
    • Cost Optimization via Evolve: In scaling these deployments to thousands of users, McCarthy Construction noted that leveraging Palantir's "Evolve" framework led to a 60% drop in LLM token usage, addressing a key operational cost barrier.

Physical Asset and Logistics Orchestration: Hertz, NScale, and McCarthy

  • NScale (AI Factory Infrastructure): NScale builds and operates massive, vertically integrated AI data centers (including 8 GW in West Virginia, 1.2 GW in Texas, and 400 MW in Portugal).
    • Supply Chain OS ("Delivery 360"): NScale utilizes Palantir to track tens of millions of components across hundreds of suppliers.
    • Automated BOM Analysis: The system uses agents to ingest messy, multi-thousand-line Bills of Materials (BOMs), instantly identifying mismatches, proposing alternate suppliers, calculating lead-time impacts, and updating financial projections.
  • Hertz (Fleet Management & Turnaround): Operating a fleet of 500,000 vehicles, Hertz deployed its "Daytona" control tower to 15,000 frontline employees, orchestrating over 4 million monthly operational touchpoints.
    • VIN-Level Automation: When an associate reports a vehicle issue (such as a broken seatbelt), an agent automatically opens a work order, pairs the incoming customer with an alternative vehicle, and suggests optimized transport paths.
    • Financial Impact: Recovering even a single percentage point of fleet utilization translates to tens of millions of dollars in annual revenue.
  • McCarthy (Large-Scale Construction): The 160-year-old commercial contractor utilizes "Pulse"—a job site mission control system—across 150 active projects.
    • Critical Path Mitigation: For complex change requests (such as an air handler unit redesign), Pulse cross-references procurement logs, models schedule impacts, and provides customized scenario options to protect the construction timeline.

Reimagining Customer Experience and Specialized Services: Parts Town and Kirkland & Ellis

  • Parts Town (Specialty Distribution): To tackle parts identification for commercial kitchen and refrigeration breakdowns, Parts Town integrated "PartsIQ" across its support teams and field technicians.
    • Real-Time Live Call Assist: AI agents actively listen to customer support calls, automatically parsing blurry images, partial serial numbers, and legacy manuals to populate customer contexts and identify parts in seconds.
    • Bottom-Line Impact: Reimagining the service lifecycle is projected to deliver over 200 basis points of EBITDA margin improvement across the business.
  • Kirkland & Ellis (Private Equity Fund Formation): Managing hundreds of billions in capital formation annually, the law firm built the "Fund Formation Engine" on Palantir's ontology to centralize decades of legal tradecraft.
    • Agentic Term-Sheet Drafting: Specialized agents collaborate to draft term sheets, extract precedent clauses, and automatically draft side-letters based on negotiated parameters.
    • Obligation Tracking: Post-close, the engine tracks complex, decade-long obligations, automatically generating checklists and draft communications for fund managers looking to make time-sensitive investments.

Securing Infrastructure and National Interests: Accenture and the USDA

  • Accenture (Software Estate Protection): Accenture manages thousands of IT estates globally, orchestrating security for over 700,000 internal employees.
    • Security Forge: Accenture uses this agentic harness alongside Palantir’s ontology to scan codebases, classify vulnerability severity, and push fixes directly into developer environments.
    • Apollo Deployment: Upon confirming a threat, Palantir's Apollo platform initiates automated, blue-green rollbacks to secure versions across thousands of production environments, reducing incident-to-remediation times from days to minutes.
  • U.S. Department of Agriculture (USDA Modernization): The USDA overhauled hundreds of fragmented, legacy IT systems that cost nearly $1 billion annually to maintain.
    • One Farmer, One File: Operating on the "Landmark" platform, the USDA consolidated agrarian data into a single, unified source of truth to support secure, digital-first farmer services.
    • Unprecedented Capital Deployment: Utilizing the ontology, the $11 billion Farmer Bridge Assistance program went live instantly, distributing $4.4 billion to row-crop producers in its first five days and breaking all historical records for online USDA enrollment.
    • National Security & Farmland: The USDA's "Admin Command Center" leverages this data fusion to proactively identify foreign investment risks and track ownership of American farmlands.

We were founded 2 years ago to meet this exact challenge. We design, build, own, and operate AI factories. And we're vertically integrated, which means we control the campus, the building, the power, the cooling, the network infrastructure, and the GPU deployment as a singular system. So, why is this hard? Why did we call Palantir? First, scale. A data center is multiple buildings, a building is several halls, a hall is hundreds of racks, and each rack has roughly thousands of parts. Multiply across seven active sites, and you are tracking tens of millions of components against thousands of purchase orders and a supplier base in the high hundreds. Second, the hardware is moving really fast. Every new chip generation comes with new power and cooling requirements. To address this, NScale is pioneering modular design. This means that our halls are engineered to accept new chips, so when NVIDIA moves from Blackwell to Rubin to whatever comes after Rubin, we can accept the new generation without having to redesign the entire building around it. Finally, you have scarce components with really long lead times. Items like switchgears and transformers have to go into procurement before the final design is locked. So we're placing orders far in advance, knowing that specs will change. And when they do, those revisions cascade through the entire delivery chain. The industry's standard answer to this has been an old ERP, a lot of asset management software, a lot of project management software, and like an army of expeditors with an ungodly amount of spreadsheets. And so, to meet the ambition that we have and our obsession with execution, we knew we had to do something very different. We had to leverage AI to build for AI. And actually, I have to tell you that before I joined NScale, I spent nearly a decade at Palantir, building with some of the most complex organizations on Earth. And it was a hugely informative experience, as you can imagine. And I came to NScale knowing one thing above all else to be true, which is that for agents and humans to work together, to really work together, they need a common ontology. So we started with the outcome and we built backwards from there, using Palantir. We started with our supply chain operating system, which we internally call Delivery 360, and I'm really pumped to show you the first two things that we built. Imagine I'm a solutions architect. My job is to manage everything that goes into building a data center, captured in a bill of materials, what the industry calls a BOM, which is a great name. You can think of it as a highly specific list of ingredients. And that list can change for any number of reasons, usually due to a customer request. And just to bring this to life, a minor tweak in a network design could mean you have to order 4,000 extra cables. So, a revised BOM lands in my inbox. On the left, you can see that the agent is immediately getting to work on trying to understand this new BOM. These BOMs are messy; they include thousands of lines. And it's a really great workflow for an agent because the agent can traverse the ontology, looking at things like SKUs and compatibility checks. It is—it is not a raw text diff. As you can see here, there are a bunch of changes. The agent is putting them into two piles. The first pile is based on everything the agent knows—what can we just automatically accept? And that pile saves me a load of time. The second pile are changes that an engineer like me has to review. And in the example here, you can see there's a mismatch in this top-of-rack switch. So as an engineer, I can understand the implication of moving between this one switch and the other. I'm okay with this change. But I don't necessarily understand the implications of the switch on timelines and budget. The agent is one step ahead of me, looking through the ontology, the purchase orders, and understanding the implications of moving from the old switch to the new one. It surfaces three recommendations for me. First, I can add this new switch to my existing order—same supplier, same delivery date. It's going to cost me half a million dollars. So I ask the agent to do some homework. I tell it to go figure out where I can put this old switch in inventory; maybe I can use it at a different site. So it goes off and does some homework. Meanwhile, I'm looking at my other two options. I can amend my existing purchase order, take out the old switches, and put in the new ones. I don't have to spend any extra money, but my delivery date gets pushed out by 9 days. Third option: cancel the order entirely, put in a new order with a new supplier, but I have to take a risk on the delivery date. It probably won't meet my SLAs. It doesn't feel like a good option. While I'm thinking about this, the agent is doing homework. It comes back and it says, "Actually, I've consulted all the other build schedules and we can send this old switch to a different site." Great. Option one is clearly the best. I'm going with that one. The switch is handled, the order is placed, and I can continue working through this inbox that would have taken me 5 to 10 times more time than it does now. And the real key thing here is that this decision gets codified into the ontology. So that judgment call compiles into every future agent decision making, meaning it gets surfaced to every single engineer in the company. But good orders don't mean anything if they don't arrive on time. And so, our—our second use case was around delivery, and we used the same ontology, the same foundation, and we built this other workflow in a fraction of the time. So, what you see behind me is our delivery dashboard, and it helps understand and mitigate the impact of delayed deliveries. We have 13 shipments in flight, and they're mapped against build schedules in real time, which is very cool. We have a 92% on-time rate. Great. And one shipment at risk. In this case, a power supply module is delayed by 12 days. The agent has already traced the cascade and identified that a customer date will slip. Since the agent already knows the approved vendor list for the site, the performance history of those vendors as it relates to this particular part, as well as current supply chain disruptions and things like weather patterns, it suggests an alternate supplier. There's a cost premium of 42k. It's a trade I'm willing to accept because I know that if we do, I can get this back on track, and I can deliver on time. The agent drafts the supplier communication, and the Gantt has already updated on screen. So let's dive a bit deeper into the ontology that's powering this vehicle agent. As a maintenance manager is reviewing some of the details we've just seen, they begin planning for the work that is coming into their shop. And they can see the vehicle's current location, upcoming preventive maintenance schedules, diagnostic information, previous rentals, moves, or camera scans on the vehicle. This enables the team to start thinking about recovering our cost, but also ensuring that work is bundled when it hits our shop. A full reliability visit extends the time between visits into our shop and avoids future downtime. With an end-to-end view of the vehicle state, this allows the humans in the loop to influence change of actions as they are needed. As work is planned, it's time to arrange the delivery of the vehicle. The agent has produced three options. One, a priority move to our internal repair shop. Two, holding the vehicle in its current position for later. Or three, moving this vehicle for vended maintenance. For each option, the agent again references the ontology, searching for additional context on part availability, transportation labor, maintenance capacity, and ensuring delivery to a new future assignment. The selected action has modeled cost, time to execute, and revenue opportunity. In real time, a decision is made on priority, and it is ready for review by our transportation team. The transportation manager is now notified that prioritization of this vehicle is needed. And they are the next action to resolve the issue reported from our associate. Understanding the current location of the drivers and the move scheduled for their workforce, the manager is able to slot this move at the beginning of an employee's shift. Driving efficiency and throughput of their staff, while ultimately reducing vehicle downtime and increasing vehicle availability for the rest of the organization. Historically, operators would optimize decisions for their department goals. But now, they're making decisions that optimize the entire system. I think of Palantir as customer number one for Apollo. We built it for ourselves. We've been working on it for the last 10 years. It's how we ship all of our software. Right, so if you have Foundry and AIP, you're using Apollo. Yeah, it's the infrastructure that allows us to ship our software. It's how you take the code written by an engineer out in Palo Alto, how you get it deployed, whether that's on a cloud stack in something like AWS, or on the back of a Humvee. There's a lot of continuous delivery solutions out there. Most of them, and in most enterprise contexts, the way you ship software is you write some code, you package it up in a container, and then once it's packaged, you go into your staging environment, you click install, and you test it out, maybe it sits there for a day, and then you go to your production environment, you click install in like some console somewhere, and your code then gets deployed into that environment. Apollo is really built around what we call release channels. And a release channel is a declarative definition for an environment that says, "I want this environment to have this behavior. I want it to take the latest version of every piece of software. I want it to be one week behind so that it has the most stable version of every software," or some kind of permutation of those different, you know, strategies for deploying software. Which then allows our customers to pick the right level of risk for what they're trying to do, for which workflows and other things. It allows you to really customize that deployment. It allows the forward deployed engineers to get the latest versions out for the right use cases, but hey, this is a stable version. So it allows me to really customize my stack, my risk profile, and everything through Apollo. To put that kind of a sense of scale on it, one of our development stacks I was looking at yesterday, there's 3,000 products, which is roughly equivalent to a Helm chart in Kubernetes, deployed on that stack. And about once a minute, a new change is pushed to that environment—a new version, a config update. The volume of permutations of 3,000 products with one change per minute is—is... Yeah, it's wild. The scale is crazy. We have, I think, one of the most complicated deployment structures in the world. We have cloud stacks, we have edge stacks, we have, you know, our—our Titan trucks where we're shipping literally onto a physical truck, mobile command outposts, all over the place. We have order—like hundreds of substrates we ship to. Apollo lets you ship to any of those. They can be connected, where you're always pushing out changes if it's an internet-connected system, or an Apollo spoke that's disconnected, you can actually still push out changes to those when they gain connectivity. So you can have disconnected edge nodes, you can have a data center, you can have a node in a factory that's, you know, running a computer vision model and you're using Apollo to ship to that. Please welcome Chief Information Security Officer from Accenture, Chris Burkhardt. Everyone, welcome back from the coffee break. My name is Chris Burkhardt. I've been with Accenture for a long time, but this is an exciting new phase. We're starting our next chapter of our relationship with Palantir, and I'm going to share a little bit today about that. I'm going to speak about Security Forge and Apollo. To begin with, Accenture's software landscape is complex and sprawling, not just across our applications but across the teams and operations that really underpin it. And we don't just manage our own estate. We manage thousands of estates in partnership with some of the largest commercial and government organizations globally—customers who face increasing velocity and volume of threats enabled by frontier models and the rest of AI. Doing this at our scale has historically required massive amounts of human and software orchestration. Imagine 700,000 Accenture employees stitching together ticketing systems, asset inventories, vulnerabilities and threat feeds, and team rosters, applying tens of thousands of patches and changes daily. Working with Palantir, we've begun our own journey to ontologize our entire estate. We can see our digital world as a connected system, not only the software and infrastructure but the teams responsible for each component, the response actions tied to incidents and vulnerabilities, and the business outcomes those components support. Every asset is linked to its owner, its dependencies, and its remediation path. This ability to leverage the ontology to scale security triage really offers the responsiveness needed for a first line of defense. But it's no longer sufficient just to react. Adversaries are pointing both frontier and open-source models at our attack surface, using them to try to subvert codebases, reason through exploit chains, and weaponize newly disclosed vulnerabilities within hours, rather than weeks. The window between public disclosure and massive exploitation has collapsed, and capabilities that were once the domain of only the most skilled threat actors are now commoditized and running at machine speed. I'm therefore excited to share how we are tackling this new frontier with Palantir. Security Forge is the agentic harness that's enabling us to combine large language models with the ontology, deploying agents against the logic of the enterprise, and identifying and acting on issues across our entire codebase. We begin by building the framework for agents to review the attack surface, with the goal of intercepting the specific offensive techniques employed by our adversaries. Each pipeline chains together multiple specialized agents, each with their own set of instructions, tools, and context to conduct a specialized security review. These pipelines are the basis of the protection layer. Much like data pipelines would pipe context into enterprise applications, these pipelines are taking the codebase as input, applying agentic logic to conduct a deep investigation, and generating remedial actions as output. Once we've built the pipeline, we can then run it across the breadth of the codebase ontology, selecting the model's goals and governance safeguards for the given task. Our security review pipelines, like all workflows on AIP, are model-agnostic. The resilience and protection of the state is not constrained to one model family or at risk of becoming outdated. As adversaries become increasingly capable, this agility is key for us to stay ahead. The pipeline really matches how a human engineer would review the codebase with tooling to identify vulnerabilities. Security Forge harnesses the ontology and the large language models to scale this effort without compromising on quality or speed. As the run continues, we see the full chain of the agent's investigation. Every single log is captured in the ontology. It's necessary for audit and governance, but it also serves as a compounding value asset, feeding best practices and known bad patterns back into our engineering org, so each next line of code is more secure than the last. With the run complete, we're presented with the findings of our agent's investigation. This then immediately triggers the evaluation layer to review the findings, consolidating duplicates and scoring severity. The ontology logs really become key here, with every step of the severity scoring and common weakness enumeration being captured. This compiles in detail how the agent identified the vulnerability, what the business context was that informs this common weakness, and all known occurrences that require remediation. But identification and early warnings are only as valuable as one's ability to act on them. Built on a foundation of human-in-the-loop and AI teaming, Security Forge immediately passes identified vulnerabilities to the humans who own the affected applications for adjudication and review. All the context is available to those users, right down to the underlying logic that the agents used to detect the issue. This enables a seamless and thorough human-in-the-loop review, with any feedback from the user captured in the ontology and made available to future agentic executions. After confirming a finding, the engineer can then move directly into remediation with the ability to copy the command-line tools into the IDE of choice and connect the Security Forge MCP server to their environment. This will pull over the exact findings, the code locations, and the remediations from the ontology right into their coding session, enabling rapid response to the vulnerabilities. But here's the magic: we can also automate this chain. The same tools our engineers would have to enact remediation can also be handed to our swarm of agents to take forward. Automated remediation is available to spin up, resolve downstream fixes, manage dependencies, and handle refactors. This scales not just the identification of the issues, but the deployment of the solutions as well, which has always been one of the hardest parts. Now that we've started the remediation process, we need to ensure the entire fleet is secured ASAP. Enter Apollo. Apollo enables us to manage the complexities of our software estate in a proactive manner. As soon as Security Forge confirms the finding, it sends those vulnerability tags to Apollo, which immediately issues a recall to all the environments where that package version is deployed. This rolls us back to safe versions while ensuring that dependencies on that package in all those environments are still maintained. As soon as remediation is complete and the new version of the impacted entity is pushed, it is picked up by Apollo, rolled out to the fleet autonomously, and all expected guardrails and processes are followed. Blue-green deployment, soaking periods, and anything that you'd normally expect from an operations team, Apollo does. Apollo adheres to your custom policies, shaping itself to your software development lifecycle and not forcing you to design your operations around it. This full loop would typically take humans working across engineering, infrastructure, and support days to complete. It's time that's no longer afforded to the commercial world given our advanced adversaries. We can also harness the same methodology beyond just security remediation. It could be performance improvements, feature requests, or customizations for our apps and for those of our customers. This is empowering Accenture to embrace the same forward-deployed engineering that you've seen other Palantir customers benefit from today. In summary, Palantir brings the software. The ontology turns a sprawling, complex estate into a single connected system. Security Forge loops through threat hunting and finding vulnerabilities at speed and scale, and Apollo deploys and updates the infrastructure to remediate those. Accenture brings the expertise: decades of running mission-critical applications and infrastructure for the largest organizations in the world, tens of thousands of patches and changes daily, and the operational judgment to know what to harden and in what order. Neither alone is sufficient. Software without domain expertise is just a platform waiting for someone to wield it. And expertise without the right software can't keep pace with adversaries operating at machine speed. Together, we've got something that neither of us could do on our own: complete domain assurance. This is not a dashboard explaining your exposure after the fact, but a living system that finds, adjudicates, remediates, deploys, and observes, so the estate hardens faster than it can be attacked. This is what the mission requires. The deep expertise of Accenture and the tooling of Apollo and AIP come together to stay ahead in the fight. Thank you. Every day, we help customers keep mission-critical equipment running. When a commercial kitchen goes down, when a refrigeration unit fails, or when a technician is working against the clock to get the kitchen back up and running, we are on the call. And across all of those situations, one challenge appears again and again for us, and that's parts identification—getting the right part to the right person at the right time. It sounds simple, but in reality, customers often don't have complete information. You can imagine that we get a blurry image, a partial part number, or a partial serial number. For nearly 40 years, our people have helped build expertise solving these specific problems. But as we continue to grow, we began asking a different question: how can every individual at Parts Town harness that expertise, not just those with years of tenure? And what if we could repeatedly compound that expertise, improving our operations and customer service with every single conversation? This isn't about taking our people out of the equation; it's about empowering them to leverage the very best of Parts Town to deliver an unparalleled customer experience. That question has become the foundation of our partnership with Palantir. And we had to begin by meeting our people where they're at, on the front line of customer support and on the phones. Our journey therefore began with PartsIQ, a tool deployed to our customer support teams to solve the core challenge of parts identification. By bringing together equipment information, manuals, service records, images, years of transcripts from customer calls, and historical knowledge, PartsIQ helps teams identify the right part faster and with much greater confidence. While on the phone with the customer, the support agent starts typing any details they receive. Whether it's manufacturer information, an incomplete part number, or a rough description of a part, the ontology starts working for them, scoping down to the relevant model to help the agent drive the conversation with that particular customer. PartsIQ was never designed to replace expertise; it was designed to amplify it. We're intentionally not replacing our people with AI agents; we're delivering our people the tools that they need to deliver the best possible support without losing what makes us uniquely great at Parts Town. PartsIQ traverses the ontology from the identified model to retrieve all relevant parts. At this point, PartsIQ has the context to start proactively guiding the user to that very specific answer. Partner, an agent operating against our ontology, jumps in to ask questions to scope down that very specific search, traversing the ontology context to expedite that resolution. Now, the customer isn't asking questions; they're assisting our agent in doing the work for them. That's a fundamental change for us. With minimal back and forth, customer support has identified the relevant part in seconds with the assistance of our new PartsIQ tool. The system will further steer the user to the most likely part, given its knowledge of how often these parts are deployed in the field and the full context of past support events. This context is all deeply linked in the ontology, and therefore traversable across our agents. With this all tightly integrated with the ontology, successfully identifying the part triggers two very key results. One, the support agent can now directly create a quote for the replacement to forward the workflow and close out the issue with that customer. And equally, if not more importantly, this resolution is captured in the ontology alongside any tribal knowledge that was used to inform that decision. That compounds our field context and further enhances the customer experience for the next caller. So PartsIQ changed the game for our people on the phones, but working with our Palantir forward-deployed engineers, we realized we could inject LLMs plus the ontology further up in the process. What if AIP could sit on the call with our people and proactively support them in delivering the best possible customer experience without losing that unique Parts Town experience? That was the inception of our work across our Unlimited Service Group, our division of 1,800 technicians in the field fixing commercial food equipment. Step one was to further transform the support process with agents working actively to support our people on the phone, backed by the same ontology that made PartsIQ so powerful. Rather than waiting for the support teams to type in queries, AIP is already capturing the conversation and guiding next steps based on our unique SOPs, our CRM data, and captured tribal knowledge. Customers can share photos, they can provide symptomatic pieces, and they can send appliance details—all of this is being actively captured by the AI agent in real time. Keep an eye on the right of the screen: the AI agent isn't just capturing the information, it's actively using it to populate the user's screen live on the call, proactively presenting them with the right customer information based on details shared on the call, and then suggesting next steps to accelerate the path to resolution. Those next steps appear directly in the user's chat window to facilitate a seamless chat with the customer. And of course, this is backed by a rich ontology, with all the relevant links to that identified customer immediately available by searching around that object. In seconds, we've identified the full history with a customer, all of their equipment, all of their warranty information, and more, without the user having to input a single search item, leaving them focused on what they're uniquely positioned to do: work with the customer to solve that problem. This is game-changing technology for us. With the additional details clarified, we dial in on further critical information: warranty details, equipment history, and everything conducive to the optimal support experience for that particular customer. But as we extend further down the decision chain, we realized we can push this initiative even further. Together with Palantir, we began reimagining the service lifecycle from end to end. Starting with a pilot inside one of our largest service brands, we worked side-by-side with the technicians, the dispatchers, and the operational leadership to build a solution from the ground up with our forward-deployed engineers, who have been absolutely fantastic from Palantir. We're able to compound on our existing ontology, extending this to the technicians' operations in the field: work orders, route optimization, and proactive service recommendations. The input is the same—the customer calls with a problem—but now the dispatch engine analyzes a work order in the context of thousands of similar ones that came before it. So two techs across branches, across the country can share the same historical knowledge base; this is a massive unlock for us across our different brands. We can recommend parts and inventory, best approach the job, route that technician with the most relevant experience to the job, and help maximize that fix rate on the very first visit we make. We again have agents working on behalf of our people, but we've massively expanded that scope, traversing all techs, their experiences, specialties, and availability to find the right individual for every work order. Here we see the agent working with the ontology context to identify a tech, prepare the work plan, and finalize the scheduling, completing all back-office work to get that technician to the customer who needs them as soon as possible. But of course, human and AI teaming means our skilled professionals are still in the loop. If they want to make a change to a schedule item, they jump into the same schedule our AI agent was writing earlier. Operators can leverage the best of the agent's work, but still make edits and inject their own unique context. The impact of this work extends far beyond technology. Across both PartsIQ and our ongoing USG use case, the projected value opportunity for our business exceeds 200 basis points of EBITDA margin—that's massive at scale. But the most important outcome is measured in capability, not in dollars. Today, Parts Town is building an intelligence platform that helps our people do what they do best: serve customers, solve problems, and make better decisions faster than ever before. What began as a parts identification challenge has become a broader transformation of how Parts Town fundamentally operates the business. Together with Palantir, we're not simply building tools; we're creating a new operating model where expertise, data, and AI work together. It is one that helps our people move faster, helps our customers succeed, strengthens our partnerships, and positions Parts Town to lead the future of specialty distribution. And we're just getting started. Thank you. Please welcome Chief Information Officer of the United States Department of Agriculture, Sam Berry. Hi, I'm Sam Berry, Chief Information Officer at the most important civilian department in the federal government: the Department of Agriculture. Going all the way back to the creation of our country, the founding fathers were passionate about the criticality of agriculture in national sovereignty, security, and even morality. President George Washington famously said, "It will not be doubted that with reference to either individual or national welfare, agriculture is of primary importance." To this day, agriculture remains a foundational element to our nation's success, both domestically and geopolitically. Look no further than the current conflict in the Middle East and its disruption to the fertilizer supply chain. Countries that are minimally self-sufficient are left in a state of emergency, while the United States is able to adapt and persevere. This self-sufficiency is not by accident, and it requires our constant attention to defend and protect as a national security asset. In that effort, it is important for us to realize that our most consequential battlefield is invisible. It resides in the networks, machinery, and data that power the core infrastructure of our nation, including the agriculture industry. Prior to this administration, the technology behind USDA's most important systems was stuck in a different era. There were hundreds of fragmented legacy systems, none of them designed to talk to each other. Here's how that translates to our farmer-facing systems. What you are looking at is an architecture that costs nearly a billion taxpayer dollars a year to support: thousands of contractors, hundreds of systems, and a cybersecurity challenge, to put it mildly. An environment like this makes it impossible to deliver a positive experience to our citizens. Fragments of farmer and farm records are duplicated and distributed across dozens of systems with little to no communication. USDA previously attempted to transform this, and it resulted in a ten-year modernization project that ran hundreds of millions of dollars over budget. One audit reported to Congress that an estimated fifteen percent of what was promised actually got delivered. The people inside USDA will tell you that that number is actually much closer to five percent. So, hundreds of millions of dollars were spent across multiple administrations with essentially zero progress. That is the estate that our current administration inherited. Thankfully, we have a team with private-sector experience who is willing to focus purely on outcomes to rebuild our systems with speed and precision using modern, state-of-the-art technology. We started at the foundation, because all of our problems were rooted in the fact that data was everywhere. This wasn't just a case of building pipelines; we had to start at the meta-level and define the semantics for our department. For example, what are the different attributes, documents, and historical information that define a farmer? What are the congressionally-required details and programs? Then we did the same exercise for a farm, and then the same exercise for a payment. These are all actually much more complex than you would expect. We realized that we were faced with an impossible task, and that the only way we could deliver on our mandate to reduce cost and improve security would be to take an entirely new approach. That is what led us to Palantir. With Palantir, we centralized USDA's core data assets under a single, unified model of reality. One record, one source of truth for each farmer. That's Palantir's ontology, and at the USDA we call it "one farmer, one file." It's the difference between data that sits in systems and context that actually understands the mission it serves. And this, of course, was key to delivering the latest technology. Everyone wants to implement AI and deliver stronger, more powerful solutions. But we know that pointing an LLM at hundreds of disconnected, ungoverned databases gets you a system that hallucinates, is insecure, and is unauditable. For something as consequential as our nation's agricultural data, that is not just useless—it's dangerous. The ontology has been the key to delivering AI-enabled technology to every farmer in the country. The LLMs will now be able to wield this governed model of reality—real farmers, real acreage, real programs—with permissions and lineage built in. On that foundation, we built Landmark. Landmark is the platform powering "one farmer, one file." It cuts through the red tape farmers face when they need something from USDA. It gives them and our staff digital-first tools they can use from home, reducing the time to complete tasks such as acreage reporting and payment certification, improving our overall ability to support the farmer without complicating the process. Here's the principle behind it: we're bringing this technology directly to the Americans who grow this country's food—people who would otherwise have little or no access to such tools. For a farmer, every hour of daylight matters. They shouldn't have to leave their land to receive critical services. Acreage reporting was a natural first area of focus, as it is something that nearly every farmer interfaces with. It is also the foundational data component to our larger national picture. This enables us to better understand our agricultural position so that we can respond to adverse events, provide intelligent recommendations to farmers on what crops will yield most successfully, and develop tooling which will detect foreign ownership of our farmlands. All of this is done while delivering a much better experience to farmers and saving them hours or days of time. We are currently deploying Gear, the tool that you see behind me, to county offices across the country to support USDA staff in reporting acreage in partnership with farmers. This streamlines the process, allowing users to subdivide land and report and certify acreage in one spot. For the first time ever, farmers will soon be able to report their own acreage independently, or work with county staff to do it on their behalf. So, we started with the operational side of agriculture, meeting farmers where they needed us today. But concurrently, we were able to quickly build on that same ontology to deliver major new initiatives like the Farmer Bridge Assistance program. For the first time ever, farmers could certify their information from the comfort of their home with a single click. One farmer told us that he submitted his application on his phone while boarding an airplane. This was the fastest deployment of capital to farmers in the history of this country. It was an eleven billion dollar program that provided payments to row-crop producers to stay solvent during market volatility. The program offered immediate cash flow relief to secure operations for the planting season while long-term, market-driven policy improvements are implemented. Within sixty-two minutes of opening, the program broke all prior USDA records for online farmer signups. Farmers were able to enroll without visiting a county office. It delivered over 4.4 billion dollars directly to farmers in the program's first five days. This wasn't a case of just building a new front-end portal for farmers to apply. Instead, this utilized all the connectivity of the ontology, enabling us to orchestrate the delivery of this program across multiple stages, the respective teams, and processes. Here we see one of those applications moving through the whole process. Palantir enabled us to connect these stages end-to-end, with human operators and technology working in parallel to ensure critical reviews were completed efficiently and accurately. Some steps are fully automated—the repetitive, mechanical work that used to take days. Others, like the state office review, are intentionally done by a person. The human review isn't a bottleneck we failed to remove; it's a checkpoint that we chose to keep. When you're deciding whether a farmer gets paid, someone is accountable for that decision. And every one of those decisions is logged and auditable. The platform allows us to control automation and apply it where it is needed. The result? Applications don't sit waiting. Actions, reviews, and updates are timely, and our farmers get what they need faster than ever before. Then, of course, this has to scale. Thousands of farmers flow through the process, all orchestrated on Landmark. Auditability is critical here: every action taken against an application is logged and can be audited if needed. We're running this process with better visibility and accuracy than ever before, without having to compromise on speed. So, you've seen how we were able to rapidly solve long-standing challenges for our department and deliver transformational programs to every farmer in the country. Now I want to talk about what's next and how solving these challenges unlocks USDA to start thinking about delivering truly next-generation tooling to every American farmer. Jumping back to acreage reporting, Landmark is already reducing the time it takes for county offices to report acreage and support our most critical programs. Now we're looking at how we can make this even more seamless with AI. Landmark Vision represents the next era, where farmers simply capture an image of their reporting documents and vision models extract all the details from the report. The crop type, plant date, irrigation status, and fields can all be captured using models, making it easier for farmers and staff to input information. Critically, this is built within our existing ontology context. This ensures the models are deployed with the appropriate guardrails and evaluations to ensure incorrect or infeasible information isn't captured. That connected ontology means that as soon as the details are extracted, all downstream actions and workflows are automatically kicked off, minimizing the need for the farmer to do any manual data entry. Workflows like this are positioning USDA for the future, and this is only the start. Let's take a step back and think about the system as a whole. A platform like this only works if the people it serves trust it. How farmers' data is used and protected is of paramount importance to us, and it is a significant part of why we're working with Palantir. Building everything on a single governed model means security isn't a wall we put around hundreds of fragmented systems. Instead, it's built into every aspect of the ontology. The same permission model that keeps our AI workflows evaluated and accurate is what protects the farmer. Every workflow, every integration, and every piece of farmer data is more secure than it has ever been, and every action can be traced. That same approach extends to a much larger national security mandate. The same ontology powers what we call the Admin Command Center: centralized visibility across the department to recognize risk before it reaches the food supply. Tracking who owns and who is buying US agricultural land used to be a slow, manual process. We're digitizing it and giving USDA clear visibility into land ownership so risk is identified quickly and proactively. Here we get a full view of the US agricultural landscape while also having visibility into threats and scenarios that could harm farmers and their ability to provide food. For example, we are able to produce comprehensive reporting on the impact of a potential drought. These signals equip USDA with the data we need to ensure our food supply remains resilient in every scenario. You've seen what is possible with this incredible technology—the pace that we can move when efficiency and outcomes are the focus, how operations can be transformed, and how the entire posture of our nation can shift through AI and proper data orchestration. But this is only the start. We still have a lot of work to do inside of USDA and beyond. Zooming out to the state of our republic, we have an imbalance in what we are capable of doing and what we are actually doing. In many ways, decadence and abundance have led to excessive corruption and waste. We have forty-plus million Americans dependent on state-sponsored food programs. Large corporations are poisoning our food supply in favor of profits. Politicians are pushing our country towards global dependence while allowing foreign adversaries to buy up our land. Intellectualism and technology are distracting our society and removing us from what it truly means to be human—to be one with the land, and to be one with how we nourish our families, communities, and nation. We have work to do. We need builders and innovators to activate and become excited about partnering with the government and serving our country. We need technology companies to operate as if every decision is life or death, leading to motivated, vibrant, hard-working cultures that develop products like what you see coming out of Palantir. Federal technology providers and their affiliates, their integrators, and their contractors must stop treating the government as a permanent opportunity for cost-overrun modernization efforts that too often lead to nowhere. Instead, they must pursue federal business with foundationally AI-enabled platforms, with lean engineering teams, and with rapid and aggressive deployment timelines. Learn from Palantir, and our country will be stronger, more secure, and more capable because of it. As an American, right now is the greatest time to be alive. We have the unique opportunity of being on the cusp of a golden age; all that we have to do is make it happen. We are on the frontier of AI. We have a president, a cabinet, and a general rising political class that is relentlessly pursuing American dominance and independence. We have a younger generation that is hungry, innovative, and ready to build like we've never built before. Data fusion and AI make every single person more capable. USDA is equipping farmers with the advanced technology they deserve, improving the resiliency of their operations while keeping our great nation secure. Thank you. Please welcome Head of Corporate Development of Palantir, Sasha Spivak. Another round of applause for our morning speakers. AIPCon 10 makes it sound like I can say something like looking back on a decade of AIP, when in reality, the first AIPCon was only three years ago. And in fact, it was meant to be our fourth FoundryCon. In that time, it felt internally like we were sprinting to deliver against a vision of an entirely new era: to deliver a new platform, and to deliver against what our customers needed. What we were actually doing was building on 15 years of investment into the Ontology. And today's moment, and in fact, every moment at Palantir, feels somewhat similar. We're able to sprint in the moment because we've been running a marathon for years. And the group of customers that we have the privilege of serving are the same way. Your tradecraft is built over years. It's hard-earned, it is highly specific, and it is an asymmetric advantage. Everything we do is in service of amplifying that tradecraft and amplifying that advantage. So even more than the gratitude that we feel for you sharing your stories, is the gratitude we feel for you doing the work itself. We know what it's like for the work to be hard.
