Treasury Sec. Scott Bessent testifies before the House Ways and Means Committee — 6/4/2026

CNBC Television(3.4M) ·261:15 ·7.6K views · 2026-06-04
Scott Bessent (Treasury Secretary)
Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent defended the administration's pro-growth tax policies and the newly launched "Trump Accounts" program before the House Ways and Means Committee, while clashing with Democrats over persistent inflation linked to the on...
TL;DR Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent defended the administration's pro-growth tax policies and the newly launched "Trump Accounts" program before the House Ways and Means Committee, while clashing with Democrats over persistent inflation linked to the ongoing Iran conflict...

TL;DR

Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent defended the administration's pro-growth tax policies and the newly launched "Trump Accounts" program before the House Ways and Means Committee, while clashing with Democrats over persistent inflation linked to the ongoing Iran conflict and a controversial, now-defunct tax audit settlement.

***

Domestic Tax Policy and the Working Families Tax Cuts

  • Tax Relief Outcomes: Secretary Bessent testified that the Working Families Tax Cuts delivered approximately $325 billion in total tax relief during the recent filing season, boosting the average individual tax refund by more than 11%.
  • Demographics of Relief: Over 62 million tax returns (44% of all filers) claimed at least one of the administration's key tax cuts, which include deductions for car loan interest, enhanced senior benefits, and tax exemptions for tips and overtime.
      • Income Distribution: Just over 70% of filers who received a tax cut earned less than $100,000 annually.
      • Tipped Workers: Under the "no tax on tips" provision, 90% of the beneficiaries earned less than $100,000.
      • Overtime Incentives: The "no tax on overtime" provision allowed hourly workers to deduct up to $12,500 (or $25,000 for married couples), with 75% of claimants making under $100,000.
  • Small Business and Capital Expensing: Committee Republicans highlighted the permanence of the Section 199A small business deduction (20% pass-through rate) and 100% bonus depreciation for capital investments.
      • The "Slingshot Effect": Bessent defended immediate expensing for new manufacturing and agricultural structures, arguing that front-loaded tax deductions quickly translate into a massive expansion of domestic productive capacity and higher future corporate tax revenues.
      • The 10-to-1 Regulatory Rule: The Treasury reported that the administration shattered its goal of cutting 10 existing regulations for every new one issued, achieving a 129-to-1 ratio in 2025.

Trump Accounts and Child Shareholder Initiatives

  • Universal Market Exposure: Designed to address the fact that nearly 40% of Americans have no exposure to U.S. equities, the "Trump Accounts" initiative aims to make every American child a shareholder.
  • Enrollment and Funding: Approximately 6 million accounts have been opened to date, with 1.4 million children eligible for a $1,000 seed contribution from the Treasury. The program is scheduled to go live on July 6, 2026.
  • Philanthropic Mobilization: The initiative has secured significant private backing, highlighted by a $6.25 billion donation from the Michael and Susan Dell Foundation.
      • Targeting Disadvantaged Communities: The Dell Foundation's contribution will provide $250 per account to eligible children, purposefully excluding the wealthiest 20% of ZIP codes in America.

Economic Growth, Inflation, and Global Energy Markets

  • The Inflation Debate: Democrats sharply criticized the administration's claims of a "golden age economy," pointing to a 3.8% headline inflation rate, a 50% spike in gasoline prices, and Q4 2025 GDP growth that fell to 0.5% (well below the projected 5.4%).
  • The Treasury's Counter-Argument: Bessent countered that core inflation has dropped to 2.8% (down from a peak of 6.6% under the Biden administration). He attributed the recent price spike in fuel and groceries to a temporary shock caused by the Iran war, promising that prices will subside as crude markets stabilize.
  • Employment and Wages: Bessent emphasized that the U.S. has averaged 2.6% GDP growth under President Trump and saw consecutive monthly real wage increases in the private sector for every month except April 2026.
  • Russian Energy Sanctions & Waivers: Bessent defended a recent 30-day extension of waivers permitting the purchase of Russian seaborne oil. He explained that the waiver was requested by vulnerable global allies during World Bank/IMF week to prevent extreme energy shortages, and argued it has kept global fuel prices lower without yielding incremental revenue to the Kremlin.

DOJ Settlement Controversy and IRS Oversight

  • Status of the "Anti-Weaponization Fund": Chairman Jason Smith and Secretary Bessent addressed the controversial Trump audit immunity settlement. Bessent confirmed that the Department of Treasury is following the Department of Justice's guidance, noting that Acting Attorney General Todd Blanche testified that the government will not move forward with the fund.
  • Democrat Backlash: Representative Lloyd Doggett and other committee Democrats accused the administration of orchestrating a corrupt deal to grant President Trump unprecedented immunity from tax audits. They highlighted the abrupt resignation of Treasury General Counsel Brian Morrisey and questioned whether career IRS employees who opposed the deal would face retaliation.
  • Refusal to Comment: Bessent repeatedly declined to provide details on the settlement, the role of IRS CEO Mr. Bizignano, or the existence of prior taxpayer audit immunity recommendations. He cited ongoing litigation and IRS Rule 6103, which strictly prohibits the disclosure of sensitive taxpayer information.

***

Notable Quotes

"Trump Accounts represent a profound reimagining of [private ownership]. They will ensure that every American child can benefit from private ownership and compound growth. That every American child, in short, is born a shareholder."

— Scott Bessent, *U.S. Treasury Secretary*

"Between the tariffs, the Iran war, prices are rising faster than they have in years... 77% of the American people disagree right now with the economic positions that are being embraced by the administration."

— Richard Neal, *Ranking Member (D-MA)*

"I call it the slingshot effect. So, for one or two years, I think we're going to see this burst of factories and manufacturers that were encumbered under the Biden administration, and I think going forward, we are going to see a breakout in corporate income tax receipts."

— Scott Bessent, *U.S. Treasury Secretary*

"This is the kind of corruption that would permit the Trump family... to pay them or not pay them as he feels it's smart to do. He made no complaint about the audits in his petition, and yet suddenly out of nowhere, he is granted immunity in what some have said... would be a $100 million benefit to him."

— Lloyd Doggett, *U.S. Representative (D-TX)*

Our trade deficit with China fell 32% last year to the lowest level since 2004. The Chinese recently agreed to purchase at least $17 billion annually in US agriculture products through 2028, alongside more Boeing airplanes. Consumer sentiment is currently at a record low. The savings rate for Americans has dropped to 2.6%, cut in half in just a year. When we look at this economy, we see failure upon failure. The policies that have been proposed have really not worked for average Americans. Families are getting crushed, and we would suggest today that a new course of action needs to be taken. Republicans have made it clear that they don't want to put a stop to the chaos, but Democrats are unwavering in our fight for American families to lower costs and to end the harm that is being done. Thank you, Mr. Chairman. Thank you, ranking member. Today's sole witness is United States Secretary of the Treasury, Scott Bessent. The committee has received your written testimony and it will be part of the formal hearing record. Secretary Bessent, you have five minutes to deliver your remarks, and you may begin when you're ready. Good morning, Chairman Smith, Ranking Member Neal, and members of the committee, thank you for convening today's hearing. I'm grateful for this opportunity to discuss President Trump's 2027 budget, which builds on this administration's progress in unleashing a new era of economic expansion. I last appeared before this committee just a few weeks before the House passed the Working Families Tax Cuts. So, on the heels of the most successful filing season in IRS history, I'd be remiss if I did not begin by thanking the committee for helping to deliver this once-in-a-generation bill to the President's desk. This Tax Day, under President Trump, we celebrated how much more money hardworking Americans kept, not how much the government took. Over 62 million tax returns claimed at least one of President Trump's signature new tax cuts: no tax on tips, no tax on overtime, deductibility of American car interest loans, and an enhanced deduction for low- and middle-income seniors. All told, the average refund increased by over 11%, with total refunds increasing by 18%. But as important as what the legislation achieved, it is what it prevented. If opponents to the Working Families Tax Cuts had had their way, our economy would have absorbed the largest—the largest—tax hike in its history, over $5 trillion. Ninety percent of American taxpayers would have seen their standard deduction slashed, while 40 million families would have seen their child tax credit halved. Instead, this committee held the line, and the American people had a better tax day because of it. Notably, President Trump's pro-growth policies don't stop at putting more money back in the pockets of working and middle-class families; they extend to placing the American dream within closer reach of their children. For context, nearly 40% of Americans have no exposure to US equities, with no stake in the companies they helped to build. Trump Accounts represent a profound reimagining of that arrangement. They will ensure that every American child can benefit from private ownership and compound growth—that every American child, in short, is born a shareholder. To date, nearly 6 million Trump Accounts have been opened, with 1.4 million eligible for the $1,000 seed contribution from Treasury. Of course, as his tax cuts deliver relief for working-class Americans, President Trump's economic agenda is bolstered by two other distinct but reinforcing levers: trade and deregulation. Let me briefly address both of these in turn. First, the President is undeterred in his determination to open markets for US goods and services while rebuilding US manufacturing capacity. Over the 12 months ending March 2026, the trade deficit for goods declined by $370 billion compared to the same timeframe ending March 2025. The economy has added 313,000 net new private-sector jobs and 13,000 manufacturing jobs in the past two months. Firm capital expenditures rose at an annual rate of over 17% in the final quarter, and companies are investing trillions to build and expand here at home. American industry is winning again, to the benefit of American workers. A whole-of-government approach, meanwhile, is coupling our manufacturing revival with a great regulatory reset. Properly calibrated regulation is essential for economic growth, capital formation, employment, and higher wages. So at the outset of this administration, President Trump set the ambitious benchmark of slashing 10 existing regulations before issuing a new one. In 2025, we shattered that goal with a ratio of 129 to 1, and as a result, the regulatory actions generated more savings last year than in those of the prior Trump administration combined. Separately, any one of these initiatives on trade, tax cuts, and deregulation would be substantial. Taken together, they are transformative. Before President Trump took office, our trading partners exploited America's markets, our regulatory state smothered businesses, and our tax code was poised to punish workers and job creators. Today, his policies are driving lower taxes, bigger paychecks, and broader prosperity. So I thank this committee for its partnership in this critical work, and I look forward to building upon our strides through the President's budget for the year ahead. Thank you. Thank you. We'll now proceed to the question and answer session. After the passage of TCJA, bonus depreciation allowed businesses to deduct the full cost of equipment and machinery immediately. But the factory building that housed the machinery had to be depreciated over a much longer 39-year schedule. This created a major gap in US tax policy. Manufacturers could quickly recover the cost of machines, but the cost recovery for the enormous capital investments in the physical factories lagged far behind. Last year's tax law extended accelerated expensing treatment to the structures of new domestic production facilities, allowing manufacturers to quickly recover the cost of the building, and in turn, make it significantly more attractive for companies to invest in expanding US manufacturing capacity. Well, if that thing gets moving, then yes, it makes our job much easier, and I would prefer that way as well, but thank you. Financial fragmentation: the $6 trillion cost of breaking the "plumbing" of global finance

World Economic Forum(1.1M) ·49:41 ·444 views · 2026-06-04
Matt Strahan (Private Market Initiatives Lead, World Economic Forum) Daniel Tannebaum (Global Anti-Financial Crime Practice Leader, Oliver Wyman) Anne Walsh (Managing Partner and Chief Investment Officer, Guggenheim Partners)
Growing geopolitical tensions and the weaponization of global finance are driving severe financial fragmentation.
TL;DR Growing geopolitical tensions and the weaponization of global finance are driving severe financial fragmentation. If a full economic decoupling between East and West occurs, the global economy faces a worst-case GDP loss of over $6 trillion (roughly 5% of global GDP)—an...

TL;DR

Growing geopolitical tensions and the weaponization of global finance are driving severe financial fragmentation. If a full economic decoupling between East and West occurs, the global economy faces a worst-case GDP loss of over $6 trillion (roughly 5% of global GDP)—an impact far exceeding that of the COVID-19 pandemic or the 2008 financial crisis.

The Drivers and Waves of Global Financial Fragmentation

The highly integrated global financial system, established after the 1971 collapse of the Bretton Woods system, relies on shared, informal norms such as the rule of law, property rights, non-expropriation of sovereign assets, and independent central banking. However, this system is fracturing across three distinct historical waves:

  • First Wave (Post-9/11): Weaponization of the Financial "Plumbing": Efforts to curb terrorist financing introduced sophisticated tools that turned global payments into instruments of economic statecraft, setting a precedent that access to the system could be restricted.
  • Second Wave (Post-2008): Erosion of Institutional Trust: The Global Financial Crisis triggered heavy regulation, reduced cross-border exposures, and normalized the aggressive use of financial stability tools by policymakers.
  • Third Wave (Post-2022): Broad Systemic Exclusion: Sanctions imposed on Russia by the G7 following the invasion of Ukraine demonstrated a willingness to exclude an entire major economy, accelerating efforts by non-Western nations to build alternative financial systems.
  • Erosion of Key Norms: Politicization of Policy and Data: Increased political interference in central bank monetary decisions and threats to the independence of government economic data damage the predictability required for international investment.

Quantifying the Cost: $6 Trillion Worst-Case Decoupling

The global financial system remains highly interconnected: cross-border bank credit stands at $40 trillion, 2023 cross-border payments exceeded $190 trillion, and governments and corporates issued more than $10 trillion in bonds in 2025. This high level of integration makes the system highly vulnerable to fragmentation shocks.

  • Low-Fragmentation Cost: Existing Trade Policies: Current tariff and trade barriers already cost the global economy over $200 billion annually.
  • Worst-Case Scenario: East-West Decoupling: A high-fragmentation scenario, where the world splits into rigid blocks aligned with either the US or China, would lower global output and raise inflation across all regions, costing up to $6 trillion.
  • Updated Model Assumptions: Revised projections from the World Economic Forum and Oliver Wyman now factor in previously unexpected realities:
      • Tariffs imposed between close allies (e.g., the US and Canada in April 2025).
      • Persistent sensitive tech trade between the US and China despite political tensions.
      • New service-sector trade restrictions in highly escalated scenarios.

Investment Impacts: Tariffs, Debt Volatility, and the US Dollar

For global investment firms, fragmentation introduces friction, limits opportunity sets, and drives market volatility.

  • Rising Trade Barriers: The Tariff Surge: In the US, average aggregate tariffs rose from 2% to roughly 11% in 2025, significantly raising cross-border transaction costs.
  • Sovereign Debt Concerns: Demanding Risk Premiums: Massive sovereign debt issuance globally, combined with policy uncertainty, has increased fixed-income volatility (as measured by the MOVE Index). Investors are demanding higher risk premiums, driving up borrowing costs for governments.
  • Shift in Dollar Assets: A Pivot to Hard Assets: While the US dollar remains dominant—accounting for 70% of global transactions—non-US investors are increasingly shifting capital away from US government debt and into other dollar-denominated hard assets like gold and silver due to US debt concerns.

The Impact on Emerging Markets and the African Continent

While advanced economies drive fragmentation, emerging markets—specifically in Africa—bear a disproportionate share of the economic fallout.

  • The "Perception Penalty": Disproportionate Risk Premiums: Emerging markets are financial price-takers. When global risks rise, African nations face restricted capital access and interest rate premiums up to 5 to 6 percentage points higher than comparable countries in other regions.
  • Compounding Pre-existing Vulnerabilities: Financial fragmentation worsens existing structural issues in Africa, including:
      • Heavy dependency on commodity exports requiring trade finance.
      • Severe infrastructure and green energy transition funding gaps.
      • Sovereign debt distress and unintegrated regional payment systems.
  • Regional Integration as a Shield: The AfCFTA Opportunity: Fragmentation is acting as a forcing mechanism for African nations to accelerate regional integration, drive domestic capital market growth, and implement the African Continental Free Trade Area (AfCFTA).
  • Technological Agility: Leapfrogging with Fintech: Free from legacy systems, African nations are highly agile in adopting digital banking, stablecoins, and digital assets to bypass fragmented global payment networks.

Mitigating the Fallout: Interoperability and Responsible Statecraft

To prevent the permanent fracturing of global capital markets, industry experts advocate for clear boundaries around economic policy and international sanctions.

  • Ensuring Payment Interoperability: Preventing Closed Networks: While alternative payment networks like China's CIPS or the Pan-African Payment and Settlement System are growing, they must remain interoperable with SWIFT. If these networks become closed, isolated "technological islands," global trade efficiency will plummet.
  • Responsible Economic Statecraft: Rules for Sanctions: To minimize unintended economic shocks (such as the 2018 US sanctions on a Russian oligarch that briefly paralyzed 10% of the global aluminum market), economic statecraft should be:
      • Targeted, time-bound, and grounded in multilateral processes.
      • Transparent regarding objectives and clear exit criteria.
      • Designed with private-sector input to mitigate downstream disruptions.

Notable Quotes

"The plumbing of finance became a lever of foreign policy, and the precedent was set that access to the system could be weaponized." — Daniel Tannebaum, Global Anti-Financial Crime Practice Leader, Oliver Wyman
"Anytime you reduce the elements or the opportunity sets for investment or you create barriers to free capital flows, you, by definition, create friction in the investment world." — Anne Walsh, Managing Partner and Chief Investment Officer, Guggenheim Partners
"African countries are facing premiums that can be up to 5, 6 percentage points higher than comparable other countries in other regions... a disproportionate level of compensation is being required relative to the actual underlying risks." — Daniel Mminele, Chairman, Nedbank
"A full economic decoupling... would cost more than $6 trillion, which is more than the impact of the global financial crisis, the COVID-19 pandemic, etc." — Matt Strahan, Private Market Initiatives Lead, World Economic Forum

In January, at the Forum's Annual Meeting in Davos, Matt and I spoke with another lead author of the report, Daniel Tannebaum, a partner and the global anti-financial crime practice leader at Oliver Wyman. Dan, thank you so much for joining Radio Davos, and joining Matt and I here in Davos, Switzerland. I want to start off by asking you to perhaps set the scene a bit. Do you mind providing a historical overview of the global financial system, and perhaps getting into the details of what happened after the end of the Bretton Woods system in the 1970s? If you step back, the system we're talking about today was essentially created after the collapse of the Bretton Woods system of fixed exchange rates in '71. What emerged over the following decades was a highly interconnected network of institutions, regulators, and technologies that enabled cross-border payments and capital flows at a scale we've never seen before. This structure, this architecture has really underpinned global growth for more than half a century. We're talking about integration, but we're also talking about fragmentation. When did the idea of fragmentation and a break apart of that system begin? When did it first emerge? So, fragmentation starts to emerge as a systemic concern in three waves. First, in response to the attacks in New York and Washington on September 11th, countries quite rightly focused on cutting off terrorist financing. In the process, they developed sophisticated tools to use the financial system as an instrument of economic statecraft, where I happened to serve at the US Treasury Department in some of those early days. Now, this mattered because the plumbing of finance became a lever of foreign policy, and the precedent was set that access to the system could be weaponized, whether for a justified purpose on which there was a broad alignment, as it was with cutting off terrorist financing after September 11th, or other purposes. The second wave came after the global financial crisis in 2008, which exposed major vulnerabilities in global banks and markets, and eroded trust in both institutions and regulators alike. It triggered regulation and a more cautious attitude to cross-border exposures, and made policymakers more comfortable using financial tools aggressively in the name of stability. And, then finally, most recently, the second invasion of Ukraine in 2022 led the G7 to impose the most significant sanctions against a major economy that we've ever seen. This showed that states were willing to use the system to exclude an entire major economy, not just targeted actors, and it accelerated efforts by others to build alternatives. Are there any good or responsible uses of economic statecraft? You mentioned a little bit about terrorist financing and combating the financing of terrorism, but what else might be an example there? So, sanctions in their most basic form as an instrument of economic statecraft were designed to force a change in behavior. Arguably, the sanctions that were imposed on South Africa related to apartheid had a hand in ultimately the lifting of the apartheid regime. Sanctions had a hand in bringing down Gaddafi and the end of his regime running Libya. Now, they're most responsible when they're targeted, time-bound, and grounded in a multilateral process. They're transparent around objectives, scope, and exit criteria, and they're designed with input from the private sector to minimize unintended consequences. The concept of positive economic statecraft doesn't really get spoken of enough because that ability to entice people to do the right thing with the carrot, more often than not, the stick is being used. When does it become harmful then to the global economy and growth? So, it becomes harmful when measures escalate quickly, including among allies with limited coordination, as we saw with Liberation Day in April, where tariffs were imposed on close US trading partners in 2025, including Canada, and a range of tariffs on African countries. It becomes harmful when tools intended for security become a routine instrument for broader economic competition, and they're extremely harmful when policymakers don't account for unintended consequences. A great example is when Russia sanctions were imposed against a host of oligarchs in 2018. There was one oligarch in question who controlled 10% of the world's aluminum. So, in essence, the US government in that designation, in that sanctioning, briefly destabilized the world's aluminum sector for a few weeks, as it took 10% of the world's aluminum essentially off the market.
