Should You Invest In Digital Credit Backed By Bitcoin?

Anthony Pompliano(645.0K) ·52:18 ·5.4K views · 2026-06-04
Matt Cole (CEO, Strive Asset Management) Anthony Pompliano (Host, Anthony Pompliano)
Digital credit—high-yield preferred equity backed by Bitcoin—is emerging as a critical transition asset to bridge the gap between fiat currency debasement and a hyper-bitcoinized future.
TL;DR Digital credit—high-yield preferred equity backed by Bitcoin—is emerging as a critical transition asset to bridge the gap between fiat currency debasement and a hyper-bitcoinized future. By eliminating maturity and equity conversion risks for issuers while providing...

TL;DR

Digital credit—high-yield preferred equity backed by Bitcoin—is emerging as a critical transition asset to bridge the gap between fiat currency debasement and a hyper-bitcoinized future. By eliminating maturity and equity conversion risks for issuers while providing double-digit yields to investors, instruments like Strive's daily-dividend-paying "Seta" are redefining institutional fixed-income portfolios.

Understanding Digital Credit and Its Value Proposition

  • The Mechanics of Seta: Seta is Strive Asset Management's flagship preferred equity security, currently paying a variable interest rate of 13% (compared to Strategy's competing Stretch product, which pays 11.5%).
      • Carry Trade Strategy: The issuer uses investor capital to buy Bitcoin, betting that Bitcoin's long-term compounded annual growth rate (CAGR)—projected by Strive at roughly 30% over the next decade—will comfortably outpace the financing cost.
  • Key Advantages for Issuers: Digital credit solves structural financing issues for Bitcoin treasury companies.
      • Maturity Risk Mitigation: It provides a perpetual liability, matching the perpetual, non-cash-flow nature of Bitcoin.
      • Eliminating Negative Convexity: Unlike convertible debt, it prevents the liability from converting to equity when Bitcoin's price surges, allowing common equity holders to capture full upside performance.
  • The "Transition Asset" Thesis: As fiat currencies face systemic debt crises, digital credit serves as a vital bridge to a Bitcoin-standard economy.
      • Volatilty Absorption: It allows risk-averse investors to participate in Bitcoin's upside via a stable, yield-bearing instrument, smoothing the transition over the next 20 to 50 years.
      • Fresh Demand Source: The success of these instruments drives secondary demand for Bitcoin, potentially accelerating hyper-bitcoinization.

Dividend Innovation and Risk Mitigation Strategies

  • Daily Dividend Payouts: Strive is positioning Seta as the first listed security in US capital markets history to pay a daily dividend.
      • Compressing Volatility: Transitioning from monthly payouts to a daily, continuous stream eliminates the massive price and volume spikes typically observed right before monthly dividend events, making the asset behave more like a money market fund or savings account.
  • Robust Cash and Asset Reserves: To survive severe market downturns, Strive maintains a zero-debt balance sheet backed by deep reserves.
      • Extended Runway: Strive holds over 12 months of cash in STRC reserves and 6 months of Stretch reserves, recently increasing its Seta dividend reserve from 12 to 18 months despite entering a bear market.
      • Bear Market Modeling: In a severe downside scenario where Bitcoin bottoms at $40,000 and remains flat until late 2027, Strive can pay all dividends purely from cash reserves without selling any Bitcoin. Beyond cash reserves, the firm holds 5 to 7 years of Bitcoin coverage.
  • Unprecedented Transparency: Strive updates its risk disclosures and metrics on its website every 15 seconds.
      • This starkly contrasts with traditional private credit, where institutional investors are accustomed to receiving opaque asset marks only once every three months.

Critiques, Target Audiences, and Corporate Treasury Shifts

  • The "Just Buy Bitcoin" Critique: Conviction-driven, volatility-tolerant investors should buy Bitcoin or amplified Bitcoin directly rather than digital credit.
      • Product-Market Fit: Digital credit is specifically designed for the vast majority of investors who are volatility-averse, require reliable cash flows, or are looking for a productive replacement for the fixed-income portion of a traditional 60/40 portfolio.
  • Lack of Traditional Debt Protections: Because digital credit is preferred equity and not debt, investors take on the maturity risk of the issuer in exchange for high double-digit yields.
  • Evaluating Michael Saylor's 32 BTC Sale: MicroStrategy’s recent sale of 32 Bitcoin was widely criticized as a "U-turn," but it represents a necessary evolution in capital allocation.
      • Optimizing Total Returns: Establishing a willingness to sell enables tax-loss harvesting and dynamic treasury management.
      • Net Buyer Reality: Despite occasional tactical sales, MicroStrategy remains a structural net buyer of Bitcoin on a monthly basis, and dynamic management ultimately reduces the risk of the firm becoming a catastrophic "forced seller."

The Competitive Landscape and the Future of Tokenization

  • Cooperative Capitalism: The rivalry between Strive (Seta/ASST) and Strategy (Stretch/STRC) is a "friendly competition" that actively grows the market.
      • Open-Source Innovation: Strive pioneered cash reserves and cleared daily dividend distributions with Nasdaq and the DTCC, creating templates that Strategy and other future issuers can replicate.
  • Preventing Single Points of Failure: A healthy ecosystem requires a highly diversified landscape of 20 to 50 issuers to prevent federal regulators or market shocks from targeting a single dominant corporate entity.
      • Path to a Digital Credit ETF: Launching a diversified, compliant 1940 Act ETF will require at least 30 distinct digital credit issuers.
  • The Tokenization Frontier: Digital credit is the ideal asset class for securitization and tokenization.
      • Challenging Commercial Banks: If tokenized digital credit is eventually integrated with debit cards and instant payments, consumers can bypass traditional bank deposits entirely, escaping depreciating fiat currencies while earning double-digit yields on their liquid balances.

Notable Quotes

"As a Bitcoiner and a long-time Bitcoiner, I firmly believe that this debt crisis will not get better and we're transitioning to a Bitcoin future. And I think digital credit could be the most important asset in this transition period..." — Matt Cole
"If you move to something like Strive is doing with Seta, where it will be the first, literally the first listed security in US capital markets history... to pay a daily dividend, then that dividend event is not an event that you really want to plan around... It should reduce and compress the volatility." — Matt Cole
"If we want our political system to allow Bitcoin, to allow us to have Bitcoin in self-custody, then having corporations—highly profitable corporations, large corporations—that have a material interest in Bitcoin succeeding is actually one of the best protective moats that self-custody Bitcoiners could have." — Matt Cole
As a Bitcoiner and a long-time Bitcoiner, I firmly believe that this debt crisis will not get better and we're transitioning to a Bitcoin future. And I think digital credit could be the most important asset in this transition period. What's going on, guys? Today we got a great conversation with Matt Cole. Matt is the CEO of Strive Asset Management, and in this conversation, we do a breakdown of digital credit. He explains what it is, how it works, what the risks are, and how they would respond to different situations. We also get into all of the pros and cons of both Strategy and Strive having these instruments in the market, and how institutional investors are responding. I found this conversation fascinating. It helped me better understand digital credit. I hope it helps you as well. Here's my conversation with Matt Cole. All right, Matt. I want to talk about digital credit. This seems to be taking the world by storm, but at the same time is creating massive controversy. Can you just describe: what is the problem that digital credit is solving? Yeah, I've been reflecting on this a lot, and I think the problem that it's solving is actually bigger than I first imagined. So maybe I'll start with what I thought the problem was that it was solving, and kind of what I think the problem is that it's solving now. When we first launched Seta, what I viewed it as was just a preferred equity security. It pays a high interest, it is backed by Bitcoin risk, and as an issuer, I was concerned with a couple of risks on the issuer side—namely, maturity risk, just because Bitcoin is such a long-duration asset, it has no cash flow, and we're trying to underwrite a perpetual bull thesis in Bitcoin. And what I wanted to do is have the longest liability I possibly could have, which is obviously a perpetual liability. Then secondarily, I wanted to remove negative convexity to the maximal degree to the upside. Because we know Bitcoin over time on average goes up and to the right—and I think it's going to go to literally infinity—I would prefer for my liability to not convert to equity when Bitcoin's ripping higher. That can constrain the total return as an issuer. So, I just viewed digital credit as a better source of financing, and I was happy to pay a double-digit interest rate to not have the negative convexity to the downside of maturity risk, and not have negative convexity to the upside of equity conversion. I just thought that was a good trade for us as an issuer, and so I wanted to be all-in on digital credit. And just coming from the fixed income world, I know how yield-starved we are. You've covered a lot, and I agree with this, the concept of the 60/40 portfolio being dead. Coming from a fixed income background and just thinking through that, it's almost like a consensus position in our circles that 60/40 is dead. But what is completely not consensus is: what do you do with the 40? Do you put it in Bitcoin? Do you put it in prediction markets? Do you put it in digital credit? What do you do with it? There are a million ideas. Do you put it in trend-following solutions? I've seen so many different ideas, a lot of interesting ideas, but I thought digital credit could make a play at that. So that was just kind of the simplistic idea to start. Where I think it's going now is something much bigger. This much bigger idea is that right now, fiat currencies are still the primary form of currency. The dollar is still the reserve currency of the world. As a long-time Bitcoiner, I firmly believe that this debt crisis will not get better and we're transitioning to a Bitcoin future. The hardest part about that transition is: how long will it take? No one knows the answer to that. I think Bitcoin continues to go up over the course of time, but does it take 5 years? I don't think so. Does it take 10 years? Does it take 20 years? Does it take 30 years? Does it take 50 years? No one really knows what that transition will look like. But when you look at other emerging countries that have had their currencies debased, what you'll see is that as that starts to happen, more and more citizens look for alternative things to use as currencies. They look to ditch the Argentine peso or whatever currency we're talking about, and I think that's going to happen in the US as well. And I think digital credit could be the most important asset in this transition period that kind of smooths it out and minimizes the volatility. People can see where it goes, but you don't have to make as hard of a prediction of when it happens, and you don't have to ride out as much volatility. What's interesting is that if that thesis plays out, and digital credit is this transition asset—maybe the ultimate transition asset, though I don't think it'll be the only transition asset—then it in and of itself could actually accelerate hyper-Bitcoinization, because you actually have fresh sources of demand coming into digital credit. So I think it plays that role. I don't think it's a forever thing. I think if we move to a hyper-Bitcoinization world, then Bitcoin becomes money, but I think digital credit could become very interesting for several decades. Now, when we think about this digital credit, can you just explain the very simple concept? You all are taking capital from investors, promising them this yield in perpetuity, and then you are buying Bitcoin. How does this work? When you describe it to someone for the first time, what do you say? Yeah, so as an issuer, it's really a carry trade. We have a cost of capital. For Seta, it's 13% right now. It's a variable rate because, as the issuer, we are trying to peg it and minimize volatility around par, which is 100, so it could go up or it could go down. For Stretch, Strategy's products, it's 11.5%. And so what we are making an investment bet on is that the average compounded annual growth rate of Bitcoin into the future will be better than that financing rate. That is what needs to be true for our common equity investors to outperform Bitcoin. Bitcoin is the hurdle rate; that has to be true. That does not have to be true for the preferred equity investor to win and to have a great asset that could pay the interest in perpetuity. The interest rate required for that to be true, for them, is much lower. So for Seta right now, it's in the neighborhood of 6.5% as that break-even interest rate where Strive could pay interest literally forever into the future. Sometimes people hear that and they say, "Oh, Strive is saying that if Bitcoin goes up 6.5%, everybody wins." No, everybody doesn't win. The Seta investor wins because they have a good credit. For the common equity investor, how it would likely play out would be that Bitcoin is going up, call it 6.5% on average, and the common equity probably largely just holds flat. It doesn't participate because all the Bitcoin returns are filtering into the preferred equity instrument. So, it's just a structured finance instrument where you have the more senior preferred and the more junior common. Now, when you think about this actual return of Bitcoin going forward, what do you think that is? Is that 20%, 30%, or 40% year-over-year, let's say for the next decade? Yeah, we have a long-term projection, call it for the next couple of decades, in the neighborhood of 30% a year. Right now, with Bitcoin in a bear market, when Bitcoin goes down, that compounded annual growth rate projection actually goes higher to make up for the bear market. And so right now it would be on the higher end of that range. I think this is a time where you want to take risk. This kind of gets into the theory of amplification for a Bitcoin treasury company. What I mean by that is that we have stated we think somewhere in the neighborhood of 30% to 60% or 70% is, for a clean balance sheet company, the range of amplification that generally makes sense and can be managed. The reason that we go to 60% to 70% actually is to say that Bitcoin going down itself could actually push amplification beyond that in the depths of a bear market. And so we ourselves as an issuer would likely stop around there, when Bitcoin's kind of at a current level right around a 200-week moving average. But I think you really want to go and be aggressive—the concept of being greedy when others are fearful—and you really want to push it to the max exactly at this period of time. On average, though, a 30% CAGR is what I think we should expect. I don't think that that's really that crazy; it tends to be right around where I think it is as well. When you look at the way that you are paying out these dividends, obviously everything was monthly. Now we see Stretch going to twice a month, and you all have gone to daily. Can you talk about the pros and cons of you guys going to daily or even twice a month? Yeah, it really gets into this epiphany I've been having of how big digital credit could be. Digital credit is a preferred equity instrument—it's not debt, it's not a money market, it's not money—but in this transitionary period, to the extent that you can make the dividend a more continuous stream of events, then what it means is that someone that holds it doesn't need to hold it waiting for the next dividend event. Right now, when it's monthly, what you'll see in the behavior of digital credit is the price of Stretch or Seta moving up to par right around the dividend event, and you'll see the volume spike, which I think is the most interesting tell. Sometimes, if you're just looking at price, you actually miss the liquidity, which I think is the most important driver of what's kind of happening behind the scenes here. And you see a massive liquidity spike because everybody wants that dividend event, and then post-dividend event, it drops on average by about the price of the dividend, but some months more and some months less. That economically makes sense; it's not anything that's crazy. But if you move to something like Strive is doing with Seta, where it will be the first listed security in US capital markets history to pay a daily dividend, then that dividend event is not an event that you really want to plan around. It's just a continuous stream. You either like Seta or you don't like Seta. And if you like it, you just hold it. You're not trying to time a dividend event, which should reduce and compress the volatility. And if you reduce and compress the volatility, then it can be used more like a money market fund, or like a savings account type of instrument. Now, when you think about what can go wrong here, the first thing that my mind goes to is: if you have to pay this in perpetuity, do you always have to pay a dividend? Could you pause the dividend? It sounds like you could lower the rate. What are some of the things that you could do to maybe mitigate risk over a very long period of time? Because a couple of years sounds great, but what about 20 or 30 years from now? Yeah, so what are the rights of the issuer? We could pause dividends, and we actually would have a fiduciary obligation as a board to pause dividends if paying a dividend would put Strive into bankruptcy risk. The next natural question is, well, what does the balance sheet look like? What would that scenario look like where something like that would be in the interest of the issuer of Strive? Seta is our flagship product, and if we were to do something that impaired the confidence in Seta, that would permanently impair the entire strategy that we're trying to execute. Obviously, as an issuer, we're going to do everything we can to not do that. But it's a fair question to ask: what does your balance sheet look like? Strive has no debt. We have 18 months of cash and marketable securities—so cash in STRC and reserves—we have over 12 months of cash, and about 6 months of Stretch reserves. What that means is that when you start to look at risk, you ask: what does downside risk look like? If you were to assume the 2022-2023 bear market played out as an example, what that would mean in Bitcoin terms today would be about a $40,000 Bitcoin bottom, and for Bitcoin to not move off of the 200-week moving average to the upside until very late 2027. That is not an impossible scenario to imagine. It is more bearish than my base case scenario—I'm pretty bullish on Bitcoin for the back half of this year—but it's not an insane scenario. If that scenario were to play out, Strive could literally do nothing and just use our cash dividend reserves, and not have to sell a single Bitcoin through that entire bear market. And then, if you were to say, well, what if Bitcoin has something worse than 2022-2023 that is longer in duration and down longer? Well, then we still have, depending on where you think the Bitcoin price is, five to seven years of Bitcoin coverage at $40,000 in and of itself. So once you start dipping into the Bitcoin, you really have to start thinking about a scenario where it's a substantially longer and worse bear market than 2022-2023 in duration and downside before we even have to touch our Bitcoin. And so it becomes very hard—not impossible, but very hard—to see a scenario where we have to actually pause the dividends. You basically have to underwrite Bitcoin failing for that to be true. When an investor buys Seta, how much of the dollar that they give you goes into Bitcoin versus going into the dollar reserve? It's a dynamic question. We're not just issuing Seta, we're also issuing the common equity, ASST. And we don't have a strict mandate to preserve the dividend reserve, so actually looking at the history of our actions is more interesting than anything I could say. Don't trust, verify. When we IPO'd Seta in November, we started with a 12-month dividend reserve. Bitcoin was over $100,000 a coin when we IPO'd Seta. We've since gone into a bear market, and so it would be natural to say, "Well, I would expect that in a Bitcoin bear market, that might be when an issuer would start to use the dividend reserve." That's probably what it's for. But what Strive has actually done is we've increased the dividend reserve from 12 months to 18 months. We were under no obligation to do so. So not only have we maintained it, we've increased it as Seta has been increasing in size. The reason is that we are actually seeing the opportunity for Seta to be bigger than we expected, and we want to do everything as an issuer to preserve confidence in this instrument to be able to withstand downside scenarios. And so, right now we feel that it's pretty appropriate to try to maintain an 18-month dividend reserve. I think it would be very possible, like if Bitcoin went down to $40,000, that we would use it. The reserve is a reserve, and if capital markets and liquidity conditions completely dried up, it should be used when needed—otherwise, what's the point of even having it? So I think it's possible we could dip into it. It's possible with Seta issuance that we often will put 100% of the money raised into Bitcoin. It's also possible that we might reserve an 18-month or a 12-month dividend reserve if we think that's the most appropriate action. But what's been actually happening is that both the common equity ASST and Seta have been firing. So it's really a dynamic question, but what is the first principle? What is the goal? The goal is to maintain confidence and maintain Seta as a low-volatility, high-yielding instrument. What that means is that if we have to reserve cash to do so, we will, but our goal is to stack Bitcoin. What do you think is the single best critique of these digital credit instruments? I think there are two different sides of the best critique. One is: why don't you just own Bitcoin? Why don't you just own amplified Bitcoin? As a Bitcoiner, I've been very open that I do not own digital credit. I own Bitcoin, and I own amplified Bitcoin exposure, because I'm so confident in the direction that this goes. That is what I view as appropriate for me and what I think is going to maximize the returns for me and my family over time, as I can ride out the volatility. I think this bear market is a bear market for ants; it doesn't even phase me. The reality is that while there are a lot of Bitcoiners that have that mindset, that's kind of like a sicko mindset. Most people are volatility-adverse. They have different needs in their life; they need cash flows, and they have a lower duration tolerance. What I saw as the problem with Bitcoin, just based on anecdotes of people I know, is that they tend to buy Bitcoin at the tops of the bull market. They don't actually have conviction, they ride off of other people's conviction and don't do the homework, and then they sell in the depths of a bear market because they weren't prepared for the volatility. And so I think digital credit provides a great product for them. But if you have the conviction and the ability, then going further out the risk spectrum is appropriate. I just think that the product-market fit for digital credit is insanely massive. On the other side, the risk-adverse side, people say, "Well, it's not debt, you don't have the credit protections." And then they'll list 50 different risks that are disclosed in the disclosures, which is fine. I think what's fair is that every individual should look at the risks of an investment, assign an expected value or probability to those, and then use those to make a risk determination. So if you think that Bitcoin's going to fail, or if you think that people and companies can't be trusted, then you will likely assign a higher expected value to those risks than maybe I would, or maybe a lot of other people would. And if you assign a higher value, then maybe you think the risk-reward of these instruments is not favorable. I think that there's really one true risk to these instruments, and the reason I say that is, as an issuer, there was one key risk that we were trying to eliminate: maturity risk. And so, if the issuer is trying to eliminate maturity risk, then that means that the investor of Seta takes that risk, and then they get compensated for that risk with the return. If that's the key risk, then you have to put an expected value on that, which we kind of went through a little bit already when we were talking about downside scenarios. You have to put a probability that you think that scenario plays out, and then evaluate what you think that means. But I think if you're willing to view Bitcoin as an asset that will likely succeed, and you need income, then I think these are a very attractive risk-reward security. Let's talk about Strategy and Michael Saylor selling Bitcoin. Obviously, they haven't done that in a number of years. They sold, now infamously, 32 Bitcoin. There are a lot of folks who are looking at recent price action of Bitcoin and saying Bitcoin went down a lot because Michael Saylor has given up. I think the Wall Street Journal called it a U-turn. It seems that there is a lot of speculation as to why this happened and what the implications are. What's your read? I think it was a necessary evolution for Strategy. When we launched Seta, in our investor calls and the IPO process, we explicitly said that if we need to sell Bitcoin, we will sell Bitcoin. It wasn't off the table, and the institutional investors really appreciated that from Strive. In call it 90% of instances, Strive has learned from Strategy. They've been the pioneers in the space. In a couple of instances, I think that we've done something that has proven to be valuable, and one of them was introducing a cash reserve when we IPO'd Seta—and now Stretch has a cash reserve also, which I think better reduces the risk profile. The second was being willing to sell Bitcoin. Saylor had been out there very publicly with a lot of messaging: "Never sell your Bitcoin, we will never sell." And so I think he just had to message that, "I'm willing to do this." That willingness to do this was always going to come with the critics saying, "Oh, this is the tip of the iceberg. First it's 32, next it's going to be 3,200." But your goal should be maximizing total returns. Bitcoin is your hurdle rate, but ultimately the goal is to maximize the total returns for your common equity shareholders. To not have selling Bitcoin on the table when it might be advantageous is not the optimal way to do that. Selling 32 Bitcoin clearly shocked the market. I think just the fact that a 32 Bitcoin sell would shock the market shows me that he had to do this to get to the point where they can operate maximizing total returns into the future. With Bitcoin down, there's potential that, just from a tax-loss harvesting perspective, more Bitcoin sales might make sense. But what's most important there is that I think he's going to be a net buyer of Bitcoin effectively every single month into the future. So, if he did a tax-loss harvest transaction and sold, whatever, 50,000 Bitcoin, and then the next day or even the next hour he buys back 50,000 Bitcoin, I think that shareholders should be thankful for that, because it actually helps them put themselves in a better long-term position. I don't think you, nor I, believe that Michael Saylor has given up on Bitcoin or has changed his views on Bitcoin, but do you think that there could be negative ramifications in terms of the story now being a little bit more nuanced? Sometimes we're buyers, sometimes we're sellers, it kind of depends on the market," the story changes a little bit. Obviously, that doesn't mean it's not a smart decision, but how do you look at story versus what is the actual right decision as a capital allocator, whether for you or for Saylor? I think the actions ultimately will speak the loudest. The story is kind of like how we talk about whether you have to have a dividend reserve: what have you actually done? I think the story becomes a little bit scary to some when you first see that first Bitcoin sell. You're on X, I'm on X, and the amount of big accounts that are predicting right now that this is the start of continual Bitcoin sales from Michael Saylor is just part of the narrative and the story for now. If you're willing to manage it on the fly, then that risk becomes substantially less. But we're in the early stages of that happening. World Labs' Fei-Fei Li on Creating Large World Models

Bloomberg Live(185.0K) ·18:22 ·153 views · 2026-06-04
Fei-Fei Li (Co-Founder & CEO, World Labs) Emily Chang (Bloomberg)
World Labs Co-Founder and CEO Fei-Fei Li is betting on "spatial intelligence" over traditional Large Language Models (LLMs), developing simulator-class Large World Models (LWMs) to bridge the gap between digital text and physical-world AI action.
TL;DR World Labs Co-Founder and CEO Fei-Fei Li is betting on "spatial intelligence" over traditional Large Language Models (LLMs), developing simulator-class Large World Models (LWMs) to bridge the gap between digital text and physical-world AI action. Beyond Words: Large...

TL;DR

World Labs Co-Founder and CEO Fei-Fei Li is betting on "spatial intelligence" over traditional Large Language Models (LLMs), developing simulator-class Large World Models (LWMs) to bridge the gap between digital text and physical-world AI action.

The Case for Spatial Intelligence and Large World Models

  • Beyond Words: Large Language Models (LLMs) cannot execute physical tasks like putting out fires or cooking. Human and animal intelligence evolved from perceiving, navigating, and interacting with the physical, 3D world.
  • The Functional Taxonomy of World Models: To clear up industry confusion, Li categorizes world models into three distinct types:
      • Renderers: Models that generate beautiful pixels on a screen (e.g., video generation). They prioritize visual appeal for human eyeballs over strict adherence to physics, dynamics, or geometry.
      • Planners: Action-oriented models used primarily in robotics that take world states as inputs and output the next logical physical step.
      • Simulators: The lynchpin of spatial intelligence and the core focus of World Labs. These models simulate 3D/4D information, semantic data, and physical laws, and can serve as the baseline for both renderers and planners.
  • Target Audience: Unlike ChatGPT's viral consumer-facing rollout, the "ChatGPT moment" for world models will likely target professional creators, industrial designers, developers, and researchers first before trickling down to consumer design tools.

Robotics and the Humanoid Reality Gap

  • Underfunding the Robotics Revolution: While humanoid robotics startups have secured $6 billion in funding, Li argues this is "too small" compared to the massive capital historical precedents like self-driving cars and LLMs required.
  • Closing the Hype Gap: Bridging the gap between humanoid promises and actual physical capability (like unloading dishwashers or retrieving packages) depends heavily on unlocking advanced simulation layers and spatial-physical intelligence.
  • Industrial and Virtual Applications: Beyond physical humanoid robots, spatial intelligence will power virtual world design, gaming, interior and industrial design, and digital twin inspections.

Cutting Through the "AI Hate Wave" and Safety Theater

  • The Rise of Public Anxiety: The current "AI hate wave" and public backlash are fueled by a polarized media landscape split between doomerism and utopianism. This void of thoughtful, moderate public discourse breeds anxiety and a perceived loss of human agency.
  • Real-World Safety vs. Safety Theater: Li advocates for scientifically grounded, responsible technology design over "safety theater" and heavy-handed regulation.
      • Healthcare Integration: Real-world, responsible AI integration is already happening quietly in hospitals, where doctors use AI for clinical charting and radiologists use it to assist in reading MRI and CT scans.
  • Educational Transformation: Because AI can now outperform average humans on standardized K-16 tests, the education system must pivot away from traditional rote-learning evaluations. Instead, educators must teach students how to use AI tools to augment their own human agency.

Rejecting "AGI" in Favor of the Scientific Quest

  • The Problem with AGI: Li avoids using the term "AGI" (Artificial General Intelligence). Instead, she focuses on the classic scientific quest defined by AI's founding fathers: creating machines that can think and act to improve human lives.
  • World Labs' Immediate Milestone: By the end of the year, World Labs plans to ship a spatial intelligence model designed to inspire entirely new, previously impossible product opportunities.

Notable Quotes

"Can words put out fires? Can words cook an omelet? ... Much of life, human life, human work life, human private life has a lot to do with perceiving, understanding, reasoning, interaction with the world including imaginary worlds of creativity." — Fei-Fei Li
"Robotics is going to be one of the most important revolution in human industrialization. Six billion dollars is too small." — Fei-Fei Li
"Right now, the oxygen, the air is all sucked into the polarized extreme of doomerism or total utopian. And when hype takes all the oxygen in the room, that void brews the kind of anxiety... because that's where real people live." — Fei-Fei Li
Everyone is focused on LLMs, ChatGPT, Claude, Large Language Models, but you have raised a billion dollars to build something different: Large World Models. Make the case for us. What is the bet you are making that others aren't? Right, so, this is my co-founded startup, World Labs, and we are all-in on spatial intelligence. The means to spatial intelligence is building a large world model. So, what is the case for us? The case for us is a 500-million-year story that animal intelligence starts with seeing and moving in a physical world. That evolution began with us as animals knowing what the world is, knowing, knowing who we are, knowing how to move around it, and interacting with it. Much of life—human life, human work life, human private life—has a lot to do with perceiving, understanding, reasoning, and interaction with the world, including the imaginary world of creativity, of, of productivity, as virtual worlds. So, unlocking that capability in machines, unlocking the capability of generating 3D, 4D worlds, unlocking the capability of reasoning within any world, unlocking the capability of teaching agents or robots, or or assisting humans to interact with the world, is what spatial intelligence is about, and that's what we are focusing on. So what can world models do ultimately that LLMs will never be able to? Can words put out fires? Can words cook an omelet? I think there's so much, right? So, we, for example, creativity—people design, whether we're designing interior space, we're designing machines, we're designing, we're designing homes, we're designing stories, so much of that is beyond words. We also use agents, whether we use agents in virtual worlds, whether it's for entertainment like gaming, or for more serious industrial, industrial applications, whether it's digital twin design, or or inspection, or or, no, or what kind of, no, many kinds of optimization tasks. Or we build robots to help us do a lot of things from putting down fires to helping healthcare scenarios to manufacturing. All these are downstream applications of unlocking spatial intelligence and building world models. So what's the, what do you think the ChatGPT moment for world models will be? Like how will we know this has arrived? Yeah, that's a great question, Emily, because chat is such a consumer behavior, that ChatGPT moment tends to be used to describe a viral, public consumer moment of getting so close to what AI can do. In a world of world models, the kind of spatial intelligence we're trying to unlock, I'm still trying to figure out if there is a corresponding consumer moment because the kind of applications we are talking about tend to be first going to the professionals: professional creators, professional designers, professional developers, professional researchers, and engineers who will use it for robotics and industrial design and all that. So, maybe we will not necessarily have a consumer moment, but maybe we will. So in the last six months, Yann LeCun left Meta to work on world models, Google shipped Project Genie, Nvidia has its own world models, Cosmos, Nvidia's also one of your investors. What do you have that they don't, and which competitors out there worry you the most? Yeah, so first of all, we started World Labs in 2024. I still remember when, when, when we were out talking about world models and spatial intelligence, it was just a year after ChatGPT. People were still totally talking about LLMs, so we, we really had a head start in understanding that this is going to be the next frontier of AI, and we were very excited by that. So, what do they have we don't? Well, first of all, I think we have an incredible team, we have the conviction— They don't have the godmother, that's for sure. But, but, but the world is big, and, and I think this is, just like LLMs, I think there will be many companies doing incredible work in world models. Just as 24 hours ago, I, we kind of got fed up that the word "world model" has been so confusing and being used in so many different ways that we actually put out a, a blog post just explaining what a functional taxonomy of world models is instead of mushing everything together. And the way I see it is right now there are three ways of calling world models when it comes to spatial intelligence. One is what I call a renderer, when the model puts beautiful pixels on the screen, mostly like a video generation model, and the consumer is mostly human eyeballs. While the model commits to beautiful pixels on the screen, it doesn't necessarily commit to physics and dynamics and geometric correctness, because that's just for human eyeball consumption, not necessarily for computation and other tasks. Then another kind of world model is what we call a planner, that is more for machines, more for robots, where it outputs—whatever the input is, the state of the world or the action—it outputs a correct action to take to the next step, and you see that kind of world model a lot for robotics applications, and you hear that in that context. The third kind, which I think is the lynchpin of the three, is a simulator. It actually is consumed by humans as well as machines, trying to respect the structure, the physics, and the dynamics of the world, and really simulate the 3D and 4D information of the world, as well as the semantic information. A simulator could become a renderer, a simulator could become a planner, but this layer is a huge, critical path, in my opinion, to unlock spatial intelligence, and that's what World Labs is working on. All of this rolls up into robotics, so I, I want to get your take on the field, and humanoids in particular. Funding for humanoids hit six billion dollars, but, you know, they still can't load my dishwasher as fast as I can, they still can't go get my Amazon packages. First of all, robotics is going to be one of the most important revolutions in human industrialization. Six billion dollars is too small, right? Just if you look at self-driving cars investment, if you look at language models investment, it took way more than six billion dollars. I'm not saying we now... I think it will take time to invest, and it will also, hopefully, not take the hype, but take the thoughtfulness to invest in the right effort. For example, unlocking world modeling and spatial intelligence and the simulation layer, all this is part of that important effort. Are we going to close the gap? I do believe World Labs is working on one of the most critical technologies in spatial physical intelligence, and obviously, that's the, that's the hope. You've been more measured on AI safety, skeptical of the doom narrative, but also of heavy-handed regulation. Makes me very boring, to be honest. I think there's just so much hype. There is so much hype. Obviously we need to build the right technology. We need to guardrail the technology, whether you use the word "responsible", you use the word "safety", or you use the word "trustworthy". Building the right technology and product so that it can empower, enhance, augment humanity and not harm them, is the goal of any work we do, whether it's AI or not. Where is it doing right? Thomas Laffont: The $4T AI IPO Wave Is Coming… and We’ve Never Seen Anything Like It

All-In Podcast(1.1M) ·32:45 ·50.4K views · 2026-06-04
Thomas Laffont (Coatue)
Coatue’s Thomas Laffont outlines an impending $4 trillion AI-driven IPO wave led by the "Magnificent Eight" (including SpaceX, OpenAI, and Anthropic), driven by unprecedented software scaling and a stark power-law dynamic where the largest "centicorn...
TL;DR Coatue’s Thomas Laffont outlines an impending $4 trillion AI-driven IPO wave led by the "Magnificent Eight" (including SpaceX, OpenAI, and Anthropic), driven by unprecedented software scaling and a stark power-law dynamic where the largest "centicorn" companies are...

TL;DR

Coatue’s Thomas Laffont outlines an impending $4 trillion AI-driven IPO wave led by the "Magnificent Eight" (including SpaceX, OpenAI, and Anthropic), driven by unprecedented software scaling and a stark power-law dynamic where the largest "centicorn" companies are compounding faster than ever before.

The Resurgence of the Unicorn Economy and the "Magnificent Eight"

  • Market Recovery: The private "unicorn economy" is rebounding, showing a 70% valuation increase on average since September 2024, stabilizing after the bloated, zero-interest-rate policy (ZIRP) era of 2021.
  • Funding Concentration: Although fewer unicorns are being minted today compared to 2021, the funding per unicorn has surged 5x. Capital is highly concentrated, with the top 10% of companies (primarily dominant AI players) capturing the vast majority of total venture funding.
  • The Magnificent Eight Index: Laffont introduces a premier private index of late-stage giants collectively valued at nearly $4 trillion:
      • *The Members:* SpaceX, Stripe, Anthropic, Databricks, Revolut, ByteDance, and Anduril.
      • *Outperformance:* This highly diversified index—spanning space tech, AI, defense, fintech, and consumer internet—has broadly outperformed the public market's "Magnificent Seven."

The $4T AI IPO Wave and Unprecedented Scaling

  • Thawing Exit Markets: The venture ecosystem is returning to equilibrium after years of high cash consumption. A massive wave of liquidity is arriving, anchored by SpaceX's upcoming secondary/IPO events and Anthropic's confidential S-1 filing.
      • Liquidity Rebalance: These listings will return more capital to limited partners (LPs) than the last 10 years of tech IPOs combined, resolving the prolonged venture liquidity drought.
  • The Unparalleled Rise of AI Scale: The revenue expansion curves of OpenAI and Anthropic are unlike any software growth cycles in history.
      • Hyperscaler Projections: After quickly eclipsing legacy SaaS giants like Workday, ServiceNow, Adobe, and Salesforce, top AI models are currently generating more revenue than Google Cloud and Azure. Projections indicate they could surpass AWS and potentially match Microsoft's entire footprint by 2028.
      • Infrastructure Funding: This trajectory is heavily capitalized by hyperscalers who are funding their own potential disruptors to secure long-term infrastructure and computing pipelines.

The "10x Paradox" and the Power Law of Scale

  • The Scale Probability Paradox: Historic data indicates that compounding advantages make it statistically easier for mega-cap companies to achieve a 10x return than early-stage startups:
      • Unicorn to Decacorn ($1B to $10B): ~8% probability.
      • Decacorn to Centicorn ($10B to $100B): 8% to 13% probability.
      • Centicorn to Trillion-Dollar Club ($100B+ to $1T+): 31% probability.
  • The Dominance Filter: Reaching the $100 billion threshold proves a company possesses a structural monopoly or durable compounding advantage.
      • The Top 10 Strategy: Chamath Palihapitiya points to historical data showing that a simple strategy of buying and annually rebalancing the top 10 largest Nasdaq companies by market cap outperforms the broader Nasdaq index by 3x over a decade.
  • Semiconductor Generational Run: Hardware and silicon providers are experiencing unprecedented growth to support this model scaling.
      • Hardware Case Study: Cerebras Systems successfully weathered a multi-year development grind before securing massive OpenAI contracts, resulting in a 5x valuation increase ahead of its IPO.
      • Memory Constraints: AI systems require massive personalization. To support this, memory requirements per user are projected to quintuple, driving structural tailwinds for global memory manufacturers.

Deconstructing the AI Revenue Model and SpaceX's Platform Play

  • Sizing the AI Ecosystem: Coatue estimates the current AI revenue market at $140 billion, projected to scale to $300 billion this year and double by 2027. This revenue is driven by three distinct pillars:
      • Consumer: Subscription fees and Average Revenue Per User (ARPU) from tools like ChatGPT Plus and Claude.
      • Advertising: Approximately 25% of digital ads served by Meta and Google are currently AI-enabled. This is expected to reach 100%, representing a $150 billion addressable opportunity.
      • Enterprise software: High-ROI developer tools and agents such as Claude Code and GitHub Copilot.
  • The SpaceX Valuation Framework: Laffont outlines how Coatue values SpaceX based on launch cadence and business model evolution:
      • *Phase 1 (Pre-constellation):* Predictable, single-use rocket launches for government entities.
      • *Phase 2 (Ramp/Constellation):* Recurring consumer and enterprise revenue driven by Starlink.
      • *Phase 3 (Scale/Platform):* Operating multiple government and military constellations, leading to platform expansion (space data centers, lunar/Mars logistics).
      • *Telco Disruption:* Starlink is directly targeting the $200B to $400B global telecommunications broadband and wireless profit pool with a structurally superior, tower-free global product.

Notable Quotes

"The winners are compounding faster than ever, which means the costs of not being in a winner are higher than ever." — Thomas Laffont
"The public market is the great equalizer... It will be the great antiseptic, it will not care about my bullshit presentation... and ultimately, I'm a big believer in the market." — Thomas Laffont
"If you bought the Nasdaq... over a 10-year period and just rebalanced every year on the top 10 companies by market cap, you outperform the index over a decade by like 3x." — Chamath Palihapitiya
"To get to that level, let's call it the trillion-dollar club, you have to have a dominant business. And then the question is just at what point do you hit saturation? And it seems like all of these markets have ended up being so much bigger than anyone would have predicted." — David Sacks

The better AI gets, the smaller its share of the economy might get – Alex Imas and Phil Trammell

Dwarkesh Patel(1.3M) ·76:09 ·23.0K views · 2026-06-04
Alex Imas (Economist) Phil Trammell (Economist) Dwarkesh Patel (Host, Dwarkesh Patel)
The economic impact of AGI will be determined by whether AI behaves like electricity (distributing downstream benefits widely) or social media (concentrating rents), making the strategic indexing of global capital gains a more vital survival strategy...
TL;DR The economic impact of AGI will be determined by whether AI behaves like electricity (distributing downstream benefits widely) or social media (concentrating rents), making the strategic indexing of global capital gains a more vital survival strategy for non-AI nations...

TL;DR

The economic impact of AGI will be determined by whether AI behaves like electricity (distributing downstream benefits widely) or social media (concentrating rents), making the strategic indexing of global capital gains a more vital survival strategy for non-AI nations than localized job-retraining programs.

Redefining Scarcity: The Relational Sector and Labor Share Dynamics

  • The Relational Sector: Alex Imas defines this as the sector of the economy where a human being in the loop is an intrinsic part of the value proposition (e.g., therapists, doctors, certain artists, and performers).
      • Scarcity and Value: Because humans are naturally scarce, value will accrue to relational services as other goods and tasks are fully automated.
      • Willingness to Pay: Imas argues that we currently lack empirical data (such as conjoint analysis) to measure consumers' willingness to pay for humans over machines in various roles.
  • The Stability of Labor Share: Historically, the labor share of the economy has remained surprisingly stable at around 60% (a "Kaldor fact"), even after massive waves of industrial automation.
      • Historical Precedent: In 1820, economist David Ricardo predicted that machine automation would lead to mass unemployment. While the specific jobs he observed were indeed automated, new service sectors emerged to maintain near-full employment.
      • Network-Adjusted Share: Phil Trammell notes that no good has been 100% automated down its entire supply chain yet; even high-tech sectors have a network-adjusted capital share of only around 50%, with human labor still adding value upstream.
  • Satiation and Capital Variety: Whether capital share approaches 100% depends on whether we "satiate" our demand for automated goods.
      • Moore's Law Counterpoint: Moore's Law can be pessimistically framed as "every 18 months, the value of computation halves" because we run out of high-value uses for it. However, if we continually find new varieties of uses for compute (as with advanced AI), capital share will keep rising without reaching a point of diminishing marginal utility.

Debunking the "White-Collar Bloodbath" and the "Messy Middle"

  • Current Labor Market Realities: Despite the rapid capabilities growth of Large Language Models (LLMs), empirical data shows virtually no evidence of macro-scale white-collar automation.
      • The Data: Research from Yale's Budget Lab shows that even in highly exposed sectors like software engineering, employment remains robust.
      • The "Junior Dev" Trend: While there is a slight slowdown in hiring growth for junior developers, overall demand for senior engineers remains high.
      • Narrative-Driven Layoffs: Imas notes that current tech layoffs are often driven by corporate peer pressure ("keeping up with the Joneses") to signal AI adoption, rather than actual labor substitution.
  • The "O-Ring" Theory of Automation: The "O-Ring" model suggests that if a job consists of ten tasks, and AI can only automate nine of them to a slightly lower standard of quality, a firm may choose to automate zero tasks to preserve the quality of the final product.
      • Reliability Frictions: In fields like law or accounting, humans remain in the loop because of regulatory frameworks, licensing, and the liability of needing a legal entity to "back up" the product.
      • The AI-Only Transition: Symmetrically, once AI becomes highly advanced, integrating humans into production flows will introduce transaction costs and reliability issues, eventually locking humans out of certain supply chains entirely.
  • The "Messy Middle" and Citrini's Recession Scenario:
      • Political Economy: Imas points out that a mere 2% increase in unemployment completely shifts the political winds. A slow "drip" of automation where workers are reabsorbed into lower-paying jobs is politically more dangerous than a sudden shock, which triggers immediate fiscal relief.
      • Negative Growth Improbability: The guests argue that a persistent recession caused by automation is highly unlikely. For abundance to generate negative economic growth, capital owners would have to entirely stop investing their wealth back into the technological frontier (e.g., data centers and fabs).

Optimal Wealth Redistribution: UBI vs. Universal Basic Capital

  • The Pitfalls of Universal Basic Income (UBI): Imas expresses strong concerns about the political economy of UBI.
      • The Power-Sharing Danger: When citizens are no longer endowed with valuable labor and must rely on a government-issued check for basic survival, they become entirely dependent on the whims of whoever is currently in power.
  • Negative Income Tax: This serves as a highly responsive insurance mechanism, creating an immediate economic floor from the day it is enacted.
  • Universal Basic Capital (UBC): Rather than redistributing cash, UBC proposes redistributing ownership shares of the companies driving the AGI transition.
      • The Targeting Problem: The core risk of UBC is portfolio targeting. If citizens are given shares of a company that eventually goes to zero (e.g., Anthropic or OpenAI) while a competitor captures the entire market surplus, the safety net fails.
  • Optimal Taxation: Traditional land taxes (Georgist taxes) may not raise enough capital in an AI-driven economy because physical land (outside of relational-adjacent locations) will not be the primary factor of production. A consumption tax (like a European-style VAT) used to purchase a broad-based basket of global equities for citizens remains a highly viable alternative.

Global Inequality: Strategic Recommendations for Non-AI Nations

  • The "Electricity vs. Social Media" Framework: The economic future of developing nations (e.g., Nigeria, India) hinges on how the gains of AGI are captured.
      • The Electricity Model (Commoditized): If AGI acts like electricity, the technology becomes a commodity. Rents will flow downstream to the users, and any nation that buys into a global index will capture the wealth.
      • The Social Media Model (Concentrated): If AGI acts like social media platforms, the rents will remain highly concentrated within a few private, frontier AI firms.
  • The Indexing Imperative:
      • The Failures of Retraining: Guests are skeptical of traditional recommendations for developing nations, such as mass education and retraining programs, given the speed of the AI transition and existing educational bottlenecks.
      • The Indexing Strategy: Trammell and Imas agree that middle-income countries should actively use sovereign wealth funds to *index* into the global AI supply chain (e.g., ASML, TSMC, Nvidia, and top software labs) rather than focusing on building localized, capital-intensive data centers.
      • Leapfrogging: Much like mobile banking bypassed traditional physical banking infrastructure in Sub-Saharan Africa, developing countries may leverage commoditized open-source models to leapfrog middle-stage economic developments.

Notable Quotes

"The pessimistic framing of Moore's Law is every 18 months, the value of computation halves... We're running out of uses of computation so fast that it's sustaining Moore's Law." — *Alex Imas*
"When [labor] is no longer the case, and we are now at the mercy of the elected official for basic needs... that to me feels like a power-sharing arrangement that's really dangerous." — *Alex Imas*
"If we fully automate the supply chains for everything else, and we satiate in everything else really fast, then the quantity of everything that's not a ballerina goes to infinity, but our marginal utility in that stuff goes to zero faster than the quantity is rising." — *Phil Trammell*

This sort of debate that we've been having actually is like 200 years old. So, David Ricardo is one of the classic economists, not neoclassical, classical economists. And if you look at Ricardo's predictions, they're actually right. If you look at all those jobs that made money in Ricardo's time, they got automated. But, but I think this elasticity of demand argument is incredibly important both for, for a lot of arguments that people make, or just a lot of labels that people use without understanding what the underlying causation is. So, people often talk about Jevons paradox. Yeah, this is this idea that as something gets cheaper, you will want so much more of it, that the total amount you spend on the thing increases, and so famously this happened to coal in Britain 200 odd years ago. But, really, this only happens if the demand for something is highly elastic. There's many things for which there is not super elastic demand. If oil, for example, gets super cheap, it's not like magically inelastic, right? Yeah, exactly. We're not going to buy more; magically there's going to be so many more cars that now we're going to be using way more oil than before. At least not in the short run. Exactly. So, the long-run elasticity is higher than the short-run elasticity. But, even in the long run, so agriculture, famously, is an example where we can produce way more food if we dedicated the same portion of the economy that we dedicated to agriculture... We're already producing more food regardless, but we could produce even more food if the same portion of the economy that was producing food 100 years ago was currently producing food. But, you know, you eat enough, and then you're done. And so, the claim with software is that it is, it is not some inherent property of markets that as it gets cheaper, you will just keep wanting more of it. It is, the claim about software is this is a particular kind of good, where as it gets cheaper, we'll want more and more of it. Google recently announced Gemini Omni and its video editing capabilities are incredible. You can upload a video and then tell Omni to do things like change the background, or adjust the lighting, or add or remove elements, all while keeping everything else consistent. But Omni isn't just a video editor. I got a chance to sit down with the research and product team behind Omni, and I learned that it's a preview of how future frontier models will be trained. It can take in any kind of input, whether that's text or audio or video. And while it doesn't currently do so, architecturally it's capable of just as seamlessly outputting images or text. So, it's really a bet on the multimodal data transfer hypothesis: the model becomes better at predicting one data type by seeing the others. For example, Omni is really good at accurately rendering text on video, even though Google didn't specifically target that capability in this model. And Omni is the next step towards more accurate world models, because in order to predict the next frame of a video, you have to have a deep understanding of physics and spatial dynamics. As Omni progresses, it'll be interesting to see whether it closes the sim-to-real gap. Because it's much harder to collect data in the real world than it is in simulation, robotics progress has lagged other applications of AI. But if you have really good video models that can simulate reality, maybe that stops being the case. And maybe that's a more general model of how goods are produced in the economy, that you've got to make sure everything is reliable and works well, and you can't automate an entire job to an AI right now, even though it might be able to perform it at some probability, you need extreme reliability in order for it to not destroy the finished good. I think this is, yeah, so this might explain why there's less automation now than there otherwise could be. But, I think it works in the other direction once AIs get advanced enough. Integrating humans into the production flow of future goods, even beyond the, even beyond the arguments about how humans will be more expensive or dumber or whatever... So even if there's some comparative advantage where it makes sense to hire a human, there will be like transaction cost and worse of a reliability that will actually make it hard to integrate humans into future production flows. One of the biggest problems in RL right now is credit assignment, because you have these extremely long rollouts and you need to know why they succeeded or failed. One of Cursor's researchers, Sasha Rush, gave me a blackboard lecture on how they used targeted RL with textual feedback to deal with this problem and train Composer 2.5. I filmed them on my iPhone, so apologies for the camerawork. First, we generate this output. It's just a sequence of tokens. We send this sequence of tokens to this model that's going to read it, and then it's going to isolate a specific token that it says is problematic. Yep. Then we're just going to do text manipulation. We're just going to take that trajectory and we're literally just going to smash in some extra tokens. After Cursor injects these hint tokens, they run another forward pass. The trajectory itself doesn't change, but the hint causes the model to assign lower probability to the error tokens. Cursor then trains the original model to match those probabilities, basically teaching it to downweight these specific mistakes. There's a lot more nuance that we couldn't include in this midroll. 10 Years of Stripe France: How enterprises pivot in the age of AI

Stripe(107.0K) ·43:23 ·453 views · 2026-06-04
Lou Grasser (Chief Digital Operations Officer, Le Monde) Carole Roth (Senior VP Hotel Experience, Accor) Matthieu Stefani (Host, Génération Do It Yourself)
As iconic French enterprises like *Le Monde* and *Accor* navigate systemic digital transitions, the rise of "agentic AI" is shifting the corporate focus from superficial interfaces to automated back-office workflows, demanding absolute executive luci...
TL;DR As iconic French enterprises like Le Monde and Accor navigate systemic digital transitions, the rise of "agentic AI" is shifting the corporate focus from superficial interfaces to automated back-office workflows, demanding absolute executive lucidity and rapid execution to...

TL;DR

As iconic French enterprises like *Le Monde* and *Accor* navigate systemic digital transitions, the rise of "agentic AI" is shifting the corporate focus from superficial interfaces to automated back-office workflows, demanding absolute executive lucidity and rapid execution to keep pace with global competitors.

Le Monde: Scaling Digital Subscriptions and Pioneer AI Deals

*Le Monde* has undergone a dramatic editorial and economic restructuring, shifting from print-centric production to a digital-first powerhouse, heavily driven by strategic AI partnerships.

  • Subscription Growth and Goals:
      • The Scale: Driven by the cultural shift toward paid subscriptions (sparked by Netflix and Spotify) and the pioneering strategy of the *New York Times*, *Le Monde* has grown to nearly 700,000 subscribers.
      • The Target: The company has set a target of reaching 1 million total subscribers, utilizing AI and international expansion to bridge the gap.
  • Pioneering AI Partnerships:
      • First-Mover Advantage: Lou Grasser (Chief Digital Operations Officer) highlighted how their CEO proactively contacted OpenAI. Within three months, they signed a landmark deal, followed by agreements with Perplexity and Meta. *Le Monde* remains one of the few global media outlets to secure direct agreements with these AI giants.
      • High-Yield Conversion: When *Le Monde* articles appear in ChatGPT, they convert readers to paid subscriptions 20 times more effectively than Meta links and 50 times more effectively than Google Discover.
  • Operational Shifts:
      • Print to Digital Reallocation: The newsroom is currently reducing the manual labor dedicated to print layout to free up journalist time for digital, audio, video, and deep investigative formats.
      • Human Quality Assurance: Despite automated translations for international markets (such as the US), *Le Monde* retains human copyeditors. Grasser emphasizes that relying solely on AI for grammar and orthography would fail their high editorial standards.

Accor: The "Asset-Light" Pivot and Tech-Enabled Hospitality

Following a monumental shift decided in 2016 and accelerated by the pandemic, hotel giant *Accor* transitioned to an "asset-light" model, focusing on brand management, franchises, and digital platforms rather than physical real estate.

  • Massive Global Scale:
      • Volume: *Accor* must fill 850,000 hotel rooms every single day across 110 countries.
      • Unification: The group unified 45 distinct hotel brands under a single loyalty ecosystem, ALL.com, to drive direct customer retention.
  • Automating the Back-Office:
      • The Administrative Burden: Currently, over 50% of hotel staff hours are consumed by billing, room allocation, and booking reconciliation.
      • The Goal: By utilizing AI to automate these friction-heavy administrative tasks, *Accor* aims to let staff focus entirely on physical hosting.
  • Redefining the Customer Experience:
      • The "School of Relationship": In the age of AI, *Accor* has launched an initiative focused on the core values of human hospitality. Carole Roth (Senior VP Hotel Experience) explained that technology like passport scanning, high-quality Wi-Fi, and AI assistants should fade into the background, leaving space for genuine human interaction.
      • Platform Complementarity: While *Accor* views booking platforms (Google, Booking.com, Expedia) as competitors, they are also essential local distributors in complex markets like Brazil, China, and India.

The Disruption of AI: From "Superficial" Use to Agentic Workflows

The panel emphasized that the true threat and opportunity of AI lies in "lucidity"—understanding that AI is not a tool for minor tasks, but an engine to entirely rewrite business workflows.

  • The Threat to Google Search: Matthieu Stefani (Host, *Génération Do It Yourself*) noted that his personal search engine usage has dropped tenfold over the past year. Users now bypass traditional search results entirely in favor of direct answers from Large Language Models (LLMs) like Claude, Gemini, and ChatGPT.
  • The Era of Agentic AI:
      • Exponential Efficiency: Companies are beginning to deploy "AI agents" that can perform complex, multi-step workflows. Stefani pointed out that in sectors like insurance brokerage, teams of just two people can now run workflows that previously required 200 employees.
      • Workflow Transformation: On Stefani's podcast, the actual recording remains 100% human and organic. However, pre-production guest research and post-production (transcription, multi-speaker recognition, automated video short creation, and cross-episode query databases) are completely automated by AI.
  • The Stripe Perspective: "Know Your Agent" (KYA): As autonomous AI agents begin executing transactions on behalf of humans (e.g., booking a trip or purchasing products based on user parameters), payment infrastructures like Stripe are moving from traditional KYC ("Know Your Customer") to KYA ("Know Your Agent") protocols to verify authorization and manage fraud.

Global Velocity: What Europe Can Learn From Shenzhen

Matthieu Stefani recently returned from Shenzhen and Shanghai, sharing stark observations on the speed of Asian technological adoption compared to Europe.

  • Fearless Execution: Chinese startups and enterprises operate in an "arena" mindset, releasing products to the market when they are only 70% to 80% ready, prioritizing speed and rapid iteration.
  • Intellectual Property Views: Unlike the Western obsession with legal patents and IP protection, the Chinese tech ecosystem views ideas as shared inspiration. If an idea is utilized by another, it is not viewed as "theft," but as a mechanism to drive collective market speed.
  • Rapid Industrial Decarbonization: Shenzhen's transition is heavily visible; approximately 80% of the city's vehicles are electric, resulting in quiet, cleaner streets—driven by necessity, a lack of local oil, and a rapidly aging population that demands intense robotization.

Announcing NVIDIA RTX Spark | GTC Taipei 2026 Keynote by CEO Jensen Huang

NVIDIA(2.2M) ·17:07 ·22.7K views · 2026-06-04
Jensen Huang (CEO, NVIDIA)
NVIDIA CEO Jensen Huang announced the NVIDIA RTX Spark, a revolutionary superchip built in partnership with MediaTek and Microsoft, designed to completely reinvent the Windows PC for the era of local, autonomous AI agents.
TL;DR NVIDIA CEO Jensen Huang announced the NVIDIA RTX Spark, a revolutionary superchip built in partnership with MediaTek and Microsoft, designed to completely reinvent the Windows PC for the era of local, autonomous AI agents. The "N1X" Architecture: Developed in close...

TL;DR

NVIDIA CEO Jensen Huang announced the NVIDIA RTX Spark, a revolutionary superchip built in partnership with MediaTek and Microsoft, designed to completely reinvent the Windows PC for the era of local, autonomous AI agents.

The Core Technology: NVIDIA RTX Spark Superchip

  • The "N1X" Architecture: Developed in close partnership with MediaTek, the RTX Spark integrates NVIDIA’s full software stack, making it 100% CUDA-compatible and capable of running all existing Windows and NVIDIA applications.
  • Hardware Specifications:
      • GPU: Blackwell RTX GPU featuring 6,144 CUDA cores and delivering 1 petaflop of AI performance.
      • CPU: A custom 20-core Grace CPU.
      • Interconnect & Memory: Fused by NVLink, boasting 128 gigabytes of unified memory.
      • Manufacturing: Built on TSMC's 3-nanometer process, packing 70 billion transistors.

Reinventing the OS and Software for AI Agents

  • The "Agentic" Runtime: Jensen Huang described the future of the PC as a shift from traditional applications to autonomous agents. Under this paradigm, Large Language Models (LLMs) act as the "modern DirectX," handling computer vision, prompts, sound, and video generation.
  • Local, Sandboxed Workflows: Agents run natively in secure sandboxes, executing complex workflows by bridging local tools with cloud-based models.
      • *Example:* A demo showcased a local agent using an OpenShell sandbox to orchestrate Rhino and Blender, automatically correcting its own design mistakes and utilizing the Flux 1 model to render photorealistic architectural mockups.
  • Adobe Partnership: Adobe has re-engineered the core architectures of Photoshop and Premiere for RTX Spark.
      • Performance: The updated suite is twice as fast as previous versions.
      • Integration: Includes Model Context Protocol (MCP) server support, allowing the software to interact natively with agents running on the laptop.

The New PC Lineup: Laptops, Desktops, and Workstations

NVIDIA and its partners are launching three distinct product families covering desktop, laptop, and workstation form factors:

  • RTX Spark Laptops & Desktops: Showcased partners like MSI. Designed for 24/7, meter-free, local agent operation. Users can run local models—such as NVIDIA's NeMoTron-3 Ultra or Meta's Llama—without cloud subscription costs or latency, connected to smart home and office security systems.
  • DGX Station for Windows: A powerhouse workstation designed for developers training and deploying large language models locally.
      • Compute Power: Delivers 20 petaflops of performance.
      • Memory Bandwidth: Features 768 gigabytes of memory and 8 terabytes per second of memory bandwidth, allowing developers to run trillion-parameter models directly at their desks.

The Strategic Vision: From PCs to Home Supercomputers

  • The "Smartphone" Pivot: Huang compared the current shift in personal computing to the transition from mobile feature phones to smartphones. Ten years from now, the PC will no longer be a device where users simply click and type.
  • The Household AI Supercomputer: NVIDIA envisions a future where homes feature dedicated AI supercomputers running continuous assistant agents.
  • Industry Alignment: 100% of the world's PC ecosystem has aligned to support and manufacture systems for the RTX Spark platform.
